Teacher Resources 216


**Grade 2 | Unit 7**
#### Introduction


This introduction includes the necessary background information to be used in teaching _The U.S._
_Civil War_ unit. The Teacher Guide for _The U.S. Civil War_ contains twelve daily lessons, each of which is
composed of three distinct parts, so that the lesson may be divided into smaller chunks of time and
presented at different intervals during the day. The entire lesson will require a total of 60 minutes.


This unit includes a two-day Pausing Point following Lesson 5. At the end of the unit, a Unit Review, a
Unit Assessment, and Culminating Activities are included to allow time to review, reinforce, assess,
and remediate content knowledge. You should spend no more than 17 days total on this unit.


~~**TEACHER RESOURCES**~~


At the back of this Teacher Guide, you will find a section titled “Teacher Resources.” In this section
you will find the following:


  - Activity Book Answer Key


  - Measures of Text Complexity


  - Texas Essential Knowledge and Skills Correlation Chart


  - English Language Proficiency Standards Correlation Chart


~~**UNIT COMPONENTS**~~


Along with this Teacher Guide, you will need the following:


  - Flip Book for _The U.S. Civil War_


  - Image Cards for _The U.S. Civil War_


  - Activity Book for _The U.S. Civil War_


  - Digital Components for _The U.S. Civil War_


All unit component materials can also be found in the program’s online materials.


~~**ADDITIONAL RESOURCES**~~


You might also consider creating a classroom lending library, allowing students to borrow
Civil War-related books to read at home with their families.


Introduction
**1**


~~**WHY THE U.S. CIVIL WAR IS IMPORTANT**~~


This unit will introduce students to an important period in the history of the United States. In an
age-appropriate way, students will learn about the controversy over slavery between the North and
the South, which was the primary issue leading to the U.S. Civil War. Within the first lesson, students
will learn that slavery is wrong and why it is wrong. They will learn about the path of freedom set
forth at the founding of the country, the influence of religious faith on those beliefs in freedom, and
how this commitment to freedom ultimately led to the civil war and the end of slavery.


Students will learn about some women and men who were significant during this time, including
Harriet Beecher Stowe, Frederick Douglass, Harriet Tubman, Abraham Lincoln, Julia Ward Howe,
Robert E. Lee, Clara Barton, and Ulysses S. Grant. Students will also learn about their motivations,
including why the abolitionists and so many others were committed to ending the practice of slavery
in the United States and around the world.


This unit will lay the foundation for in-depth studies of the U.S. Civil War in later grades. It will also
set the stage for the Grade 2 _Fighting for a Cause_ unit, which will be taught later in the school year.


This unit also provides opportunities for students to build content knowledge and draw connections
to the social studies subject area, but it does not explicitly teach the Texas Essential Knowledge
and Skills standards for Social Studies. At times throughout the unit, you may wish to build on
class discussions to support students in making cross-curricular connections to the strands of
Citizenship, Geography, History, and Social Studies Skills from the social studies discipline. For
more information on religious source material in reading/language arts, consult the program guide.
For information about the quantitative and qualitative measures used to determine the complexity
of the texts, see the Measures of Text Complexity in the Teacher Resources.


~~**WHAT STUDENTS HAVE ALREADY LEARNED**~~


The following units, and the specific core content that was targeted in those units, are particularly
relevant to the Read-Alouds students will hear in _The U.S. Civil War_ . This background knowledge will
greatly enhance students’ understanding of the Read-Alouds with which they are about to engage. If
your students did not follow the program in Kindergarten and Grade 1, it is recommended that you
review the introductory overviews from the following units to fill any gaps in students’ background
knowledge.


**• America Our Great Country (Kindergarten, Unit 10)**


**• American Independence (Grade 1, Unit 8)**


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**2**


~~**CORE VOCABULARY FOR THE U.S. CIVIL WAR**~~


The following list contains all of the core vocabulary words in _The U.S. Civil War_ in the forms in which
they appear in the Read-Alouds or, in some instances, in the “Introducing the Read-Aloud” section at
the beginning of the lesson. Boldfaced words in the list have an associated Word Work activity. The
inclusion of the words on this list does not mean that students are immediately expected to be able
to use all of these words on their own. However, through repeated exposure throughout the lessons,
they should acquire a good understanding of most of these words and begin to use some of them in
conversation.

Introduction
**3**


~~**CORE CONTENT OBJECTIVES**~~


     - Describe what slavery is and identify examples of it throughout global history.


     - Explain what the Great Awakenings were and what many Founding Fathers grew to believe about
slavery.


     - Describe what the abolitionists believed about slavery and what influenced them.


     - Identify the Underground Railroad as a system of escape for slaves in the United States.


     - Describe the life and contributions of Harriet Tubman.


     - Identify the differences between the Northern and Southern economies leading up to the Civil War.


     - Describe Abraham Lincoln’s beliefs about slavery.


     - Explain the conflicts between the North and South that led to the Civil War.


     - Explain how the “Battle Hymn of the Republic” inspired Union soldiers to keep fighting.


     - Explain the importance of the Emancipation Proclamation.


     - Explain why President Lincoln is remembered as a courageous president.


     - Describe the life and contributions of Clara Barton.


     - Describe the similarities and differences between the Union and Confederate armies.


     - Describe the similarities and differences between Robert E. Lee and Ulysses S. Grant.


     - Summarize the costs and outcomes of the Civil War.


     - Describe Abraham Lincoln’s role in ending slavery in the United States.


~~**WRITING**~~


In this unit, students will be writing entries into a Civil War journal, detailing various events and
people associated with the Civil War (Lessons 2, 3, 4, 8, 9, 10, 11, and 12). These activities may be
added to students’ writing portfolios to showcase student writing within and across units.


In this unit, students will also analyze poetry, connecting the figurative language in the poem
“Christmas Bells” to the growing conflict in the North and South before the Civil War (Lesson 5).
Additionally, students will analyze poetic language and meaning in the lyrics of the popular Civil War
era song “Battle Hymn of the Republic” to better understand how the song inspired Union soldiers
and their supporters to continue fighting in the Civil War (Lesson 7).

history. ~~**TEKS 2.6.F; TEKS 2.6.G**~~

Students will demonstrate an understanding of the Tier 2 word _value_ .


~~**TEKS 2.3.B**~~


**Writing**

Students will describe what slavery is and how it has been a problem in many

countries around the world.

Lesson 1 The Problem of Slavery
**7**


~~**ADVANCE PREPARATION**~~


**Introducing the Read-Aloud**


                 - Prepare to display and reference the Global Map during the reading or use
an alternate global map for support.

**Application**


                  - Prepare to display Activity Page 1.1. Alternatively, you may access a digital
version in the activity pages for this unit.


**Note to Teacher**


                  - This unit necessarily includes the topic of slavery. Be sure to explain that
slavery is a terrible thing that has been used by different groups of people
throughout history, going back thousands of years. Unfortunately, slavery
also existed in the past in our country, and life was very hard for enslaved
people in America. Many people in the time before the American Civil War
came to believe that slavery was morally wrong, but slavery was still allowed.
Emphasize that slavery was wrong then, and it is wrong today.


**Universal Access**

**Introducing the Read-Aloud**


                 - Assign student pairs for the Check for Understanding activity. After reading
each decision, give students a moment to turn to a partner and discuss why
they think it is good or bad. This allows students to articulate their reasoning
and learn from each other’s perspectives.


**Read-Aloud**


                 - Invite students to work in small groups during the Comprehension

Discussion. During the discussion, encourage students to engage in a

Turn and Talk to discuss the answers to the questions based on their

understanding of the Read-Aloud.


**Application**


                 - As students complete Activity Page 1.2, provide sentence starters like the

ones below to support their thinking and writing development on the topic

of slavery, why it is wrong, and where it was practiced.


                         - Slavery is _____.


                         - Slavery is wrong because _____.


                         - Slavery was practiced in _____.


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**8**


~~**VOCABULARY**~~


**Core Vocabulary**


**colonies, n.** formed when people leave their country and build a home in a
new place, and the country they came from controls the new place

Example: A long time ago, people left their old country and established
_colonies_, but the old country still told them what to do.
Variation(s): colony


**democracy, n.** a form of government where people are free to vote for their
leaders and free to decide what kind of laws they want to have

Example: In a _democracy_, people get to vote to decide who will be their
leaders.
Variation(s): democratic


**slave, n.** a person who is held against his or her will and forced to work for no
pay and does not have the freedom to make choices

Example: A long time ago, some people were _slaves_ and forced to do things
they didn’t want to do.


**enslaved, adj.** being made a slave
Example: _Enslaved_ people do not have the freedom to work and live without
being told how.
Variation(s): none


**institution, n.** an established custom, practice, or law in a society or culture
Example: The community picnic is an _institution_ where families gather every
year to share food and play games.
Variation(s): none


**masters, n.** people who controlled or owned slaves
Example: A long time ago, _masters_ told slaves what to do without letting
them decide for themselves.
Variation(s): master


**slavery, n.** an institution built around the idea that a person can own or
control another person who is forced to work for little or no pay, has little or
no freedoms, and given no choice to leave their situation

Example: _Slavery_ is unfair because all people should have the right to
be paid for their work and should be free to decide where to work and live.
Variation(s): none


**value, n.** the way in which something is useful or important
Example: Jorge’s father always stressed the _value_ of getting a good
education.
Variation(s): valued

**Start Lesson**



**Lesson 1: The Problem of Slavery**
#### Introducing the Read-Aloud



10m



**Speaking and Listening:** Students will review prior learning and understand

background information in preparation for beginning the unit.


~~**TEKS 2.1.D**~~


~~**UNIT INTRODUCTION (10 MIN.)**~~


                  - Tell students that for the next few weeks, they will learn about slavery.


                 - Ask students if they have heard about slavery.


                  - Tell students that they will learn that slavery happened all over the world
throughout history, as well as in the United States where we live.


                  - Explain that they will learn that people in different parts of the country strongly
disagreed about slavery as the United States grew and spread westward.


                 - Slavery was allowed in some states but not in others. Having different parts
of the United States disagree about whether or not slavery should be allowed
was the primary issue that led to a war.


                 - Ask students if they know what it means to go to war.


                 - Explain that this war was called the U.S. Civil War or the War Between
the States.


                  - Explain that a civil war is a war between two different groups within the
same country.


                 - Tell students that they will learn about important events and important
people related to the U.S. Civil War.


**TEKS 2.1.D** Work collaboratively with others to follow agreed-upon rules for discussion, including listening to others, speaking
when recognized, making appropriate contributions, and building on the ideas of others.

Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**10**


  - Tell students that today’s Read-Aloud is about histories of slavery around
the world.


  - Tell students that they’ll hear new words like _value_, the way in which
something is useful or important.


  - Explain that these examples of slavery happened in the past and learning
about it will help us to make better decisions.


  - Explain the difference between a good decision and a bad decision.


Check for Understanding


**Stand Up/Sit Down.** I am going to read you four decisions. After I read
each decision, turn to your partner and discuss why you think it is good or
bad. Then, on my signal, if the decision that I read to you describes a good
decision, stand up. If the decision that I read describes a bad decision, sit
down.


        - Helping a friend who is feeling sad (stand up)


        - Interrupting or not paying attention during a classroom discussion
(sit down)


        - Apologizing to a friend when you make a mistake (stand up)


        - Excluding someone from a group activity (sit down)


**Lesson 1: The Problem of Slavery**
30m

~~**TEKS 2.3.B TEKS 2.3.B**~~


~~**PURPOSE FOR LISTENING**~~


  - Tell students to listen carefully to learn about how slavery existed around the
world before it appeared in the United States.

ideas; **TEKS 2.3.B** Use context within and beyond a sentence to determine the meaning of unfamiliar words.

~~**ELPS 1.A; ELPS 1.E**~~



Lesson 1 The Problem of Slavery
**11**


Flip Book Images
1A-1–1A-8


Support


It may be difficult for
students to understand

how a person could be

bought and sold. Be
prepared to explain that a
long time ago, slaves were

treated like they were
things instead of people.
Highlight that it is not fair

or right for people to be
treated that way. Convey

that society has learned

from these mistakes
and has made changes
to ensure that everyone
has the freedom to make

their own choices and be

treated fairly and with

kindness.



~~**“THE PROBLEM OF SLAVERY” (15 MIN.)**~~


**Show Image 1A-1: Making Choices**



~~_What do you see in this picture?_~~ Every day of
your life, you make choices. Some are good and
some are bad. You can do the right thing and
choose to finish your homework. Or, you can
choose to do the wrong thing and copy another

thing is never okay. However, it gives you the
opportunity ~~_or chance_~~ to learn from your mistakes. When you face the same
choice in the future, you will have learned an important lesson and will not
repeat the bad behavior. ~~_What are some examples of good and bad choices?_~~





**Show Image 1A-2: Studying History**



One of the reasons we study history is to learn
from the past choices of other people. This
helps to inform ~~_or make_~~ our own decisions.
Some of the past choices that people made
were right. Some of the past choices people

walked the Earth, right and wrong, and good
and evil, have co-existed ~~_or happened at the same time_~~ in every village, town,
and nation. So, when you learn about history, you need the full story. You
must study the good and the bad. That way, you will have the tools you need
to make better decisions today. ~~_Why is it important to learn about the good_~~
~~_and bad things that people did in the past?_~~





Now, we are going to talk about an evil that has been with the human race for
thousands of years: **slavery** . ~~Slavery~~ ~~_is the practice of holding people against_~~
~~_their will, forcing them to work without pay, and denying them the freedom to_~~
~~_decide how to live their lives._~~


**Show Image 1A-3: Bought and Sold**





What does it mean to be a slave? ~~_A slave is_~~
~~_a person who is not free to make choices._~~
If someone is **enslaved**, it means that they
do not have the freedom to make their own
choices. They are controlled by someone
else. An enslaved person was often wrongly



Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**12**


considered to be someone else’s property and could be bought and sold for
a specific **value,** ~~_or price that the person was determined to be worth. The_~~
~~_word_~~ ~~value~~ ~~_tells us how useful or important something is._~~ Slaves throughout
history have often been treated badly by their **masters,** ~~_the people who_~~
~~_controlled or owned slaves._~~ Many had to work difficult jobs and were
separated from their families. No matter how they were treated, they did not
have freedom to leave their situation.


**Show Image 1A-4: Ancient Greece**



For most of human history, slavery was very
common. More than 4,000 years ago, in
ancient Egypt, slavery was widespread and
common. We have studied the ancient Greek
civilization, from more than 2,000 years ago.

Ancient Greece is thought to be the birthplace
of **democracy.** Democracy is a form of government where people are free to
vote for their leaders and free to decide what kind of laws they want to have.
~~_Why is_~~ ~~democracy~~ ~~_important?_~~ But even in a place that we think of as the
birthplace of democracy and freedom, there were slaves.

Have students think
about why there were
slaves in a place that had
democracy and freedom.
Invite them to give an
example of a situation
where something good
and something bad
happened at the same
time.





**Show Image 1A-5: Slaves in Ancient Greece**



Slavery was very common in ancient Greece.
In Athens, for example, slaves were imported,
or brought in, from other nearby territories.
They were captured during war, taken by
pirates, or bought from traders. ~~[Point to the~~

~~_this picture?_~~ Slaves were seen as a master’s
property. The slaves could be bought and sold. Slaves could be treated with
respect or could be treated harshly. They had a variety of jobs, ranging from
working as housekeepers to doing brutal work digging for valuable metals in
mines deep in the ground. Some historians think that well over one-third of
the population of ancient Greece was enslaved.







**Show Image 1A-6: Mali Empire**


Slavery existed long before ancient Greece,
and continued long after. As time went
on, the **institution** of slavery continued in
many different nations. ~~_An_~~ ~~institution~~ ~~_is an_~~
~~_established custom, practice, or law in a society_~~
~~_or culture._~~ Mansa Musa was the Muslim ruler

**13**


of the nation we call the Mali Empire, which existed about 700 years ago. The
Mali Empire was located in West Africa. Even today, there is a country in West
Africa called Mali. ~~[Point out Mali on the Global Map.]~~ Back in the 1300s, the
Mali Empire was very wealthy. Much of the world’s gold was found in mines
throughout West Africa controlled by the Mali Empire. Back then, 700 years
ago, people did not have refrigerators, so salt was very important to preserve
food. Mali became a major source of salt for much of the Western world. The
Mali Empire had large numbers of slaves working in these mines.


**Show Image 1A-7: Caravan from Mecca**


Mansa Musa ruled Mali when the empire was
at its height of power, 700 years ago. Mansa, or
king, Musa is thought by some to be the richest
person who ever lived. He had large amounts
of gold and salt. But he also had many slaves.

Musa took his religious journey to Mecca, he brought 12,000 slaves with him.





Slavery existed on the other side of the world, too. Before the Europeans
arrived in North America, Native Americans enslaved one another. The
journals of traders, explorers, and missionaries who later ventured across
North America described finding many Native American enslaved persons.


Many of the European nations that were establishing **colonies** in North
and South America also practiced slavery. ~~_When people leave their country_~~
~~_and build a home in a new place, but they are still controlled by the country_~~
~~_they came from, it is called a_~~ ~~colony~~ ~~_._~~ The Spanish Empire explored much of
Central and South America and set up many colonies. The Spanish colonies
in the islands of the Caribbean had large numbers of slaves. The Portuguese
Empire created a colony in what is modern-day Brazil, and that colony
included thousands of slaves. Many British colonies also had slaves. ~~[Point~~
~~out these areas on the Global Map.]~~


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**14**


**Show Image 1A-8: Lincoln Reading**
**the Emancipation Proclamation**
**Before His Cabinet**


Slavery has taken many forms and existed for
thousands of years. It has impacted people of
many different races and religions. Though it
may look different now, slavery tragically still
exists in many parts of the world today.



By the 1700s, as the 13 British colonies of North America started the first steps
to becoming an independent nation, the practice of slavery had unfortunately
become all too common in the colonies and could be very harsh. However, many
people were becoming aware that it was wrong. Many of America’s Founding
Fathers recognized how evil slavery was. Still, it would take nearly 90 years after
America became a nation and a grueling civil war for slavery to be outlawed, ~~_or_~~
~~_not allowed,_~~ with the passing of the 13th Amendment to the U.S. Constitution.
We will learn about the heroes who stood up for freedom and who secured the
rights and liberties of every man, woman, and child.


~~**COMPREHENSION QUESTIONS (10 MIN.)**~~


If students have difficulty responding to questions, reread pertinent
passages of the Read-Aloud and/or refer to specific images. If students
give one-word answers and/or fail to use Read-Aloud or unit vocabulary in
their responses, acknowledge correct responses by expanding students’
responses using richer and more complex language. Ask students to answer in
complete sentences by having them restate the question in their responses.


1. **Literal.** What does _slave_ mean?


» If someone is a slave, it means that they do not have the freedom
to make their own choices; they are held against their will, forced
to work for no pay, and controlled by someone else.


2. **Literal.** What are some places where slavery existed? (Mapping Activity:
Students use the text to map places throughout history where slavery has
occurred.)


» Slavery existed in ancient Egypt; it existed in ancient Greece; it existed
in Mali; it existed in Central and South America; it existed in America.


Lesson 1 The Problem of Slavery
**15**


3. **Inferential.** _Think-Pair-Share:_ An inference is an educated guess we can
make based on what we read in the text. What inference can you make about
what life was like for enslaved people?


» Life for enslaved people was hard. They had to work hard; they had to
do things that they didn’t want to do; they weren’t paid for their hard
work; and many had minimal shelter, clothing, and food.


Check for Understanding


**Stand Up/Sit Down.** I am going to read you six statements. If the
statement I read to you correctly describes something connected with
slavery around the world, stand up. If the statement I read does not
describe something connected with slavery around the world, sit down.


                         - Slaves were treated as property and could be bought and sold.
(stand up)


                         - Slavery was only in North and South America. (sit down)


                         - Mansa Musa, a ruler in West Africa, had many slaves during his religious
journey to Mecca. (stand up)


                         - Slavery has existed for thousands of years and has impacted people of
many different races and religions. (stand up)


                         - Slavery is still present in some parts of the world today. (stand up)


~~**WORD WORK: VALUE (5 MIN.)**~~


1. In the Read-Aloud you heard, “An enslaved person was often wrongly
considered to be someone else’s property and could be bought and sold for a
specific _value_, or price that the person was determined to be worth.”


2. Say the word _value_ with me.


3. The _value_ of something tells how useful or important it is.


4. An air conditioner keeps you nice and cool on hot days, making it of great
value during the summer months.


5. What has value to you and why? Try to use the word value when you talk
about it. [Ask two or three students. If necessary, guide and/or rephrase
students’ responses: “_____ has great value to me because _____.”]


6. What’s the word we’ve been talking about?


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**16**


**Use a Making Choices activity for follow-up.** I am going to read a phrase
about something that either has value or does not have value. If the phrase
describes something that has value, then say, “That has value.” If the phrase
does not describe something that has value, say, “That does not have value.”
(Answers may vary, as students may have different opinions about some of
these.)


  - A one-of-a-kind painting by a famous artist (That has value.)


  - A broken pencil (That does not have value.)


  - A guide dog for a person who is blind (That has value.)


  - A book with many pages missing (That does not have value.)


  - A calculator for a very difficult math problem (That has value.)


  - Food for someone who has not eaten all day (That has value.)


  - An umbrella that is full of holes (That does not have value.)


  - A flat tire on a bike (That does not have value.)


  - A coat on a cold day (That has value.)


**NOTE:** As answers may vary, remind students that the value of something
can be different for everyone. What is of value to me may not be of value to
someone else.

Activity Page 1.2



Check for Understanding


**Recall:** Ask students the following questions to help them remember
details from the Read-Aloud:


       - What is slavery?


» Slavery is the practice of holding people against their will, forcing
them to work without pay, and denying them the freedom to decide
how to live their lives.


       - Why is slavery wrong?


» Slavery is wrong because it takes away freedoms; people couldn’t
make their own decisions; people worked for no pay.


       - What are some places around the world where there was slavery?


» Ancient Egypt, Ancient Greece, Mali, Central and South America,
and more.


       - Tell students you are going to write down what they say.


       - Record this information on Activity Page 1.1. Once the information has
been recorded, read it to the class.


~~**THE PROBLEM OF SLAVERY (15 MIN)**~~


 - Give each student Activity Page 1.2.


 - Tell students that they are going to use the activity page to write three
sentences. The first sentence should tell what slavery is. The second
sentence should tell why slavery is wrong. The last sentence should name
one place in the world besides the United States where slavery happened.


 - Have students think about what they learned about slavery from the ReadAloud. Then, ask the question, _What is slavery?_


 - Have students write one sentence to answer the question. Give students
time to write.


 - When students are done writing, ask the question, _Why is slavery wrong?_
Before they begin writing, have them think about why slavery is wrong using
what they learned from the Read-Aloud. Then, invite students to write one
sentence to answer the question. Give students time to write.


 - When students are done writing the second sentence, remind them that the
last sentence should name one place in the world where slavery happened
other than the United States. Tell students to think about the places around
the world that we wrote down in the previous activity. Have students write
one sentence to answer the question. Give students time to write.

Lesson 2 The Great Awakenings and the Founders

~~**ADVANCE PREPARATION**~~


**Introducing the Read-Aloud**


                 - Use the completed Activity Page 1.1 to review what was learned in the
previous Read-Aloud about slavery and where it occurred around the world.


**Read-Aloud**


                 - Prepare to display and reference the U.S. Map during the Read-Aloud or use
an alternate U.S. map for support.

**Application**


                  - Prepare and display a version of Activity Page 2.1. Alternatively, you may

access a digital version in the activity pages for this unit.


**Universal Access**

**Introducing the Read-Aloud**


                 - During the Essential Background Information activity, assign student pairs

to discuss their responses to the given question before they share their
predictions aloud.


**Read-Aloud**


                 - During the Read-Aloud, assign small groups for students to turn and talk to

their group members when prediction questions are asked instead of sharing

as a whole group. Encourage groups to share their predictions aloud and

compare them to the predictions made by other groups.


**Application**


                 - During the “Before and After” activity, assign students to small groups.

Provide each group with a Somebody, Wanted, But, So, Then chart on chart

paper. Encourage groups to choose a preferred location in the room to

discuss their responses and record them on the chart paper. Finally, allow

each group to share their responses with the class before completing the

activity as a whole group.


~~**CORE VOCABULARY**~~


**abolish, v.** to get rid of or stop
Example: The class decided to _abolish_ homework on weekends.
Variation(s): abolished, abolishes, abolishing


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**22**


**compromise, v.** to work together to find a middle ground
Example: During recess, the students found a way to _compromise_ on which
game to play, so everyone could play together.
Variation(s): none


**First Great Awakening, n.** a strong religious movement in the American
colonies mostly during the 1720s-1740s that emphasized the ideas of a
personal relationship with God and individual liberty

Example: A long time ago, people were excited to be part of the _First Great_
_Awakening_, which taught people to live their lives by following the teachings
of the Bible.
Variation(s): none


**Founding Fathers, n.** a group of men led by George Washington that helped
the colonies separate from Great Britain to establish the government of the
United States

Example: The _Founding Fathers_ helped the colonies start a new country with
its own rules.
Variation(s): none


**govern, v.** to make decisions and rules that help a group of people live
together peacefully

Example: Teachers and school leaders help _govern_ the school by making
rules that keep everyone safe and happy.
Variation(s): none


**plantations, n.** large farms where crops to be sold are grown and harvested
that often used enslaved people for labor

Example: There were many crops growing on the _plantations_ .
Variation(s): plantation


**Puritans, n.** a religious group of people who wanted to purify the Church of
England by getting rid of practices not found in the Bible

Example: The _Puritans_ wanted religious freedom and some became
pilgrims, traveling to the New World to form a new religious community.
Variation(s): none


**sermon, n.** a speech that a pastor or preacher gives to provide religious
instruction and guidance

Example: On Sundays, families go to church to listen to the pastor’s _sermon_
about being kind and helpful.
Variation(s): none

~~**ESSENTIAL BACKGROUND INFORMATION OR TERMS (5 MIN.)**~~


 - Tell students that in today’s Read-Aloud, they’ll hear new words like
_abolish_, which means get rid of or stop. They will also hear the word
_compromise_, which means working together to find a middle ground.


 - Tell students that they will hear about something called the First Great
Awakening. Tell them to think of an “awakening” like a moment when a
lightbulb turns on in your mind and you suddenly understand something
in a new way.


 - Explain that they will learn about groups of people, including some of the
Founding Fathers, who began to change their view about whether people
should be enslaved.


 - [Reread the last sentence from the previous Read-Aloud: “We will learn
about the heroes who stood up for freedom and who secured the rights
and liberties of every man, woman, and child.] Ask students to make
predictions and answer the following questions:


    - Do you think some of the Founding Fathers will be the heroes who
stand up for freedom against slavery? [Encourage students to share
their thoughts and reasons for their opinions.]


    - What do you think these people might do to stand up against slavery?

[This question encourages students to think about possible actions
or ways these people might have taken a stand.]

2A-1–2A-9



The practice of slavery has been unfortunately
far too common throughout history. Because
of slavery, hundreds of years ago, many people
were brought on ships from Africa to North
and South America. These enslaved people

Portuguese colonies in South America, and in





**Show Image 2A-2: Pilgrims Leaving England**



In the colonies of British North America, most
people who came were settlers looking for
a better life. Many of those colonists came
looking for opportunities to create wealth for
themselves and their families. Other colonists

in those North American colonies. Regardless
of their reasons, the colonists had to **compromise** ~~_or find a way to work_~~
~~_together._~~





We’ve read about some of the people who came to America motivated by religious
freedom. The Pilgrims helped establish colonies in the North, like Massachusetts.

~~[Display the U.S. Map, and point to Massachusetts on the map.]~~ They came to
America with strong religious beliefs that required them to separate from the
Church of England and its practices. Other groups, like the Puritans, also came to
the New World to live in ways consistent with their interpretations of the Christian
Bible, not as they were required to do in Europe.


Lesson 2 The Great Awakenings and the Founders
**27**


Support


Encourage students to
reflect on the Puritans’
move to a new world.
Prompt them to consider
what it would take for them
to leave their homes and
move to a completely new
environment.



For example, one of the first governors of the
colony of Massachusetts was a man named
John Winthrop. He was a Puritan, and deeply
motivated by his religious beliefs. In 1630, he
gave a sermon, ~~_or a speech that a pastor or_~~

~~_and guidance,_~~ where he described his vision for
the new American colony: “For we must consider that we shall be as a city
upon a hill. The eyes of all people are upon us.” Winthrop was quoting from
a sermon delivered by Jesus of Nazareth as recorded in the Christian Bible.
Winthrop was telling the settlers that they must live in the way God called
them to live, as an example to the rest of the world. ~~_How do you think John_~~
~~_Winthrop wanted the colonists to live? How do you know?_~~



**Show Image 2A-3: John Winthrop**





**Show Image 2A-4: Puritan Settlers**



Slavery was still a common practice
throughout the world during this time, and
these early Puritan settlers were accustomed
to slavery like most other people. But
their religious faith and ideas about liberty

In Massachusetts as early as 1646, courts
ruled in certain cases that slavery was unlawful, even that it was a sin, or
something harmful based on their religious beliefs. While slavery was still
practiced in many places, the religious beliefs of some of these early settlers
in the American colonies planted seeds that had an impact over time. ~~_What_~~
~~_happens to a seed when you plant it? When it is said that the Puritans planted_~~
~~_seeds, it means they shared their beliefs and over time those beliefs started_~~
~~_to grow and spread to other people—just like when you plant a tiny seed in the_~~
~~_ground and it grows into a big, strong plant._~~





By the early 1700s, people from different religions were living in all
13 colonies. Many people strongly practiced their faith. But religious
interpretations of the issue of slavery were different for different Christians.
And for some colonists, religious beliefs were not as strong.

**Awakening**, and it swept across all of the
colonies. Colonists who participated in the

**Show Image 2A-6: Great Awakening**
**Preachers**



The Great Awakening preachers emphasized
their religious beliefs. This included the belief
that all people are made in the image of God
so that every person deserves dignity and

born with equal rights, given by God, to the
basic freedom of deciding how to work and live.
And it included the idea that if people were equal in the eyes of God then the
laws of the nation should treat them equally. Based on these ideas, many
people began to realize that slavery should not continue. The First Great
Awakening influenced many of those who ended up leading America’s war for
independence from Great Britain. ~~_What does it mean to be treated equally?_~~

Many of the Founding Fathers came to believe
that slavery was a moral evil that must end.

During his life, George Washington owned slaves. But like many during that
time, he grew to see the practice as morally wrong, and made plans in his
will to free them after his wife’s death. George Washington wrote in a letter





Lesson 2 The Great Awakenings and the Founders
**29**


Challenge


Why do you think the
Founding Fathers, who
believed slavery was
wrong, had a hard time
finding a solution to end it?


Support


Explain that the
Constitution is like a very
important rulebook for our
country, the United States.
The Founding Fathers
wrote it to tell us how
our government should
work and what rights and
freedoms we all have.



that he wished to see a plan to **abolish**, or get rid of, slavery. Washington
wrote, “[T]here is not a man living who wishes more sincerely than I do, to
see a plan adopted for the abolition ~~_or the ending or stopping_~~ of [slavery].”
Another Founding Father, Benjamin Franklin, became president of the

Abolition Society in Pennsylvania, proposing laws to abolish slavery. ~~[Point~~
~~to Pennsylvania on the U.S. Map. Preview that Pennsylvania is a Northern~~
~~state that fought on the side of the Union because they supported the end of~~
~~slavery during the Civil War.]~~


While attitudes about slavery were changing for many, they did not change for
everyone. The Founding Fathers struggled with finding solutions because slavery
greatly affected life in both the South and North. Southern plantations depended
on enslaved labor to produce foods like rice, as well as cotton, tobacco, and other
products needed by the North. Without enslaved labor, those businesses could fail.
They were also concerned that this issue would divide the colonies. If the colonies
could not come together in compromise, there could be no independent, free
America. And for those committed to ending slavery, without a free America, slavery
would continue in the colonies, as it was still common practice throughout the world.


**Show Image 2A-8: The U.S. Constitution**


Even though many people thought that slavery
was wrong, some people insisted that it must
continue. After fighting for independence from
Britain, the 13 new American states had to
come together to decide how they were going

~~_decisions and rules that help a group of people_~~
~~_live together peacefully._~~ Ten years after declaring
independence, each state sent representatives to a series of meetings in 1787.
This was the Constitutional Convention when they would write the Constitution
that would govern the United States. The representatives had many different ideas
on how to govern the new United States of America. The strongest disagreements
centered on slavery.





The Founding Fathers needed to walk a fine line to get all of the 13 states to agree
to adopt the Constitution. Ultimately, they made a compromise, which meant
they found a middle ground. They agreed to allow the institution of slavery but left
open the possibility that slavery would be outlawed ~~_or not allowed_~~ in the future.


During the time of the American Revolution, many people thought that slavery
was going to go away on its own. In some cases, **plantations**, or large farms that


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**30**


used slave labor to grow tobacco or cotton, were losing money. The slave trade,
where new slaves were brought to America from across the ocean, slowed and
eventually stopped completely. ~~_Why did they stop bringing new slaves across_~~
~~_the ocean to America?_~~


**Show Image 2A-9: Cotton Gin**


Then in 1794, a man named Eli Whitney invented
a machine called the cotton gin. This machine
made harvesting cotton much easier and more
profitable, which led to the growth of slavery.
The cotton gin reduced the labor of removing

for enslaved labor to grow and pick the cotton.
For the South, it meant that more cotton could
be produced cheaply for their use and to export. For the North, the cotton
gin meant more cotton for its textile mills. As a result, some people who had
slaves wanted more land and more slaves to grow and pick cotton. Slavery,
which had been around for thousands of years, did not go away on its own as
many of the Founders anticipated ~~_or expected_~~ ~~.~~ More work would be required to
ensure America truly served as the shining city on the hill.

~~**COMPREHENSION QUESTIONS (10 MIN.)**~~


1. **Literal.** What invention by Eli Whitney made harvesting cotton easier
and more profitable?


» The cotton gin; the cotton harvesting machine


2. **Literal.** What do we call the group of men who secured our independence
from Great Britain and established the government of the United States?


» The Founding Fathers


3. **Inferential.** If the cotton gin made harvesting cotton much easier,
why did the work get harder for enslaved people?


» The work got harder for enslaved people because the demand

for cotton increased. People wanted to grow more cotton to make more
money so they needed more land; more land meant that
more slaves were needed.

~~**WORD WORK: COMPROMISE (5 MIN.)**~~


1. In the Read-Aloud you heard, “Regardless of their reasons, the colonists
had to _compromise_, or find a way to work together.”

4. Two friends want to watch different TV shows. Instead of arguing, they
_compromise_ by agreeing to watch one show today and the other show
tomorrow.


5. Have you ever had to _compromise_ ? Try to use the word _compromise_ when
you talk about it. [Ask two or three students. If necessary, guide and/or
rephrase students’ responses: “I had to compromise when _____.”]

Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**32**


**Use a Making Choices activity for follow-up.** I am going to read a
statement about something that either shows compromise or does
not show compromise. If the statement describes a situation that
demonstrates compromise, then say, “That’s how you compromise.” If the
statement
does not demonstrate compromise, say, “That is not how you compromise.”
(Answers may vary, as students may have different opinions about some
of these.)


  - Sharing a toy with a friend even though you want to play with it by yourself


» That’s how you compromise.


  - Deciding to take turns with your sibling when playing a video game


» That’s how you compromise.


  - Refusing to listen to someone else’s idea and only doing things your way


» That is not how you compromise.


  - Always choosing the game or activity you want without considering what
your friend wants


» That is not how you compromise.


  - Deciding to share a bag of candy with your friend, taking turns picking

pieces


» That’s how you compromise.


  - Wanting to play outside, but your friend prefers indoor activities, so you
choose a game that can be played both inside and outside


     - That’s how you compromise.


  - Refusing to share any of your snacks with a friend during snack time


» That is not how you compromise.


  - Agreeing to help your sibling do their chores if they help you do yours


» That’s how you compromise.


**Note:** As you conduct the Making Choices activity, it’s essential to
create an inclusive and respectful environment where all students feel
comfortable expressing their opinions. Encourage students to share
their thoughts, even if they differ, and reinforce the idea that everyone’s
perspective is valued.

~~**BEFORE AND AFTER (10 MIN.)**~~


 - Display Activity Page 2.1.


 - Tell students that they are going to retell why slavery was going away
before the invention of the cotton gin.


 - Explain that we will retell together as a class, using Activity Page 2.1, a
Somebody, Wanted, But, So, Then chart, just like the one displayed.


 - Explain that this chart helps organize information about what different
groups of people wanted, the challenges they faced, and what happened
as a result.


    - Somebody: Identify the groups or individuals involved.

    - Wanted: Record what the groups or individuals wanted.


    - But: Describe the conflicts or obstacles that the group or individuals
encountered.


    - So: Describe how the conflicts or obstacles were resolved.


    - Then: Explain the impact or consequences that came after the
resolution.


 - Complete the sections one row at a time with students. When completed,
it should look similar to the one below.


 - Once the information has been recorded, read it to the class.


**TEKS 2.6.H** Synthesize information to create new understanding; **TEKS 2.7.C** Use text evidence to support an appropriate
response; **TEKS 2.7.D** Retell and paraphrase texts in ways that maintain meaning and logical order.

**Universal Access**


**Introducing the Read-Aloud**

                 - Assign students to small groups to discuss what they’ve learned about
slavery before the Check for Understanding activity.


**Read-Aloud**

                 - During the Word Work portion of the Read-Aloud, bring in images of local

businesses in the community. Highlight how these businesses contribute

to the local economy by providing goods and services, creating jobs, and

supporting the community. Assign student partners to discuss the goods

and services that their families use.


**Application**


                 - After assigning student pairs, invite students to choose how they want

to record and share their responses with the class—whether through

drawing, writing, or a combination of both to accommodate diverse learning

preferences.


~~**CORE VOCABULARY**~~


**abolitionists, n.** people who worked to abolish, or end, slavery
Example: The _abolitionists_ met often to talk about their plans to
end slavery.
Variation(s): abolitionist


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**38**


**economy, n.** the system in which people make, buy, sell, and trade items
or services

Example: Americans and people in other countries help make the U.S.
_economy_ stronger when they buy goods produced within the
United States.
Variation(s): economies


**factories, n.** buildings where goods are made
Example: Many toy _factories_ around the world produce children’s toys

for people to buy.
Variation(s): factory


**proverb, n.** a wise saying about life passed down through generations
with meaning that goes beyond what the words say

Example: “An apple a day keeps the doctor away” is a _proverb_ that tells
us eating healthy is good for us.
Variation(s): proverbs


**Second Great Awakening, n.** a strong religious movement in America
during the early 1800s following the First Great Awakening which inspired
many social changes, including increasing the opposition to slavery

Example: Long ago, the _Second Great Awakening_ spread across America
and inspired people to confront the evils of slavery.
Variation(s): none


**Underground Railroad, n.** a network of secret routes and safe houses
that helped enslaved people escape to freedom

Example: The _Underground Railroad_ was like a secret path where good
people helped slaves escape to safety and freedom.
Variation(s): none

Lesson 3 The Abolitionists
**41**


~~**ESSENTIAL BACKGROUND INFORMATION OR TERMS (5 MIN.)**~~


                - Explain that today’s Read-Aloud shows some differences between the
economy of the North and South in the years prior to the Civil War.


                 - Explain that the word _economy_ describes the system in which people make
items to buy, sell, or trade with others who want those items. When people
trade, buy, or sell goods with one another, they are cooperating. Because
of this cooperation, people can get resources, such as food, clothing, and
shelter, that they might not be able to produce or make for themselves. When
more and more people engage in trading, buying, and selling goods, we say
the economy is strong.


                - Explain that the Southern economy was dependent upon the work of slaves
to produce crops, like cotton. The North was dependent upon those crops
produced by slaves in the South for their factories, including southern
cotton needed for northern textile mills. To produce these crops, Southern
plantation owners forced slaves to work on their plantations for no pay and
often with harsh treatment.


                  - Explain that a civil war is a war between two different groups within the
same country, like the North and the South. Remind students that one of the
primary issues between the North and the South was a disagreement over
slavery.

Most farms in the South were small and run entirely by the families that lived
on them. But there were also enormous plantations where the plantation
owners who grew these crops forced hundreds of slaves to work day after
day under horrible conditions for no wages at all. ~~_What is a plantation?_~~ The
crops grown on these plantations were bought by people in the North and as
far away as Great Britain, and that helped the Southern economy grow.


The North had farms, too, but they tended to be smaller, and they didn’t
grow things that were sold to make goods. Farmers in the North grew crops,
~~_or plants that farmers grow on farms mainly,_~~ for feeding people and animals.
There were few slaves in the North.







the South. These plantations could produce the
materials at low cost because they used enslaved labor.





Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**44**


The factories in the North had access to railroads and shipping ports to
distribute the goods made there. People in the South and as far away as
Great Britain would buy goods from Northern factories, helping the Northern
economy. ~~_What were some things made in Northern factories?_~~ Manufacturing
goods to sell was the most important part of the Northern economy, whereas
farming was the most important part of the Southern economy. ~~_Why do you_~~
~~_think farming was more important in the South?_~~


**Show Image 3A-5: U.S. Map in 1850**


There were economic differences between the
North and the South. Slavery was one of the
biggest differences. Slavery became illegal, or
against the law, in most Northern states. But
even though it was illegal not everyone in the

not a part of their everyday life, some people in
the North didn’t really think much about it.



Support


As you introduce the
Second Great Awakening,
take a moment to remind
students about the
previous discussion on
the First Great Awakening
from Lesson 1.





But there were Americans who did think about slavery, and they thought
it was evil. At the time of the American revolution, many anti-slavery
Americans held strong Christian religious beliefs. After the Revolution
and through the early 1800s, a **Second Great Awakening** spread across
America, inspiring Americans to reconnect with their faith and forcing even
more Americans to confront the evils of slavery. One influential Second Great
Awakening preacher encouraged people to be against slavery: “[T]he silence
of Christians upon the subject,” he said, “is virtually saying that they do not
consider slavery as a sin. The truth is, it is a subject upon which they cannot
be silent without guilt.”


These Christians were absolutely against slavery, no matter what it did
for the economy. These people saw slavery as evil; they thought slaves
should be free and have the same rights as other Americans. People who
worked to abolish, or end, slavery became known as **abolitionists** . ~~_What did_~~
~~abolitionists~~ ~~_want to end?_~~ This group of abolitionists continued to grow larger
and larger over time. Abolitionists wrote to the people in government and held
meetings and conferences. They also gave speeches, printed newspapers and
books, and wrote songs. Abolitionists worked hard to tell everyone how bad
slavery was. They made sure people couldn’t forget about it.


Lesson 3 The Abolitionists
**45**


**Show Image 3A-6: Harriet Beecher Stowe**



In 1852 Harriet Beecher Stowe published
the novel _Uncle Tom’s Cabin_ . Stowe was the
daughter of a famous preacher, and she
was a strong abolitionist. As Stowe writes
in the book’s preface, she set out to awaken

struggles faced by slaves and did just that
through the story in her book. Her story showed that enslaved people were
humans with emotions and drew attention to the harsh things slaves were
forced to endure. ~~_Why was it important to show that enslaved people were_~~
~~_humans with emotions?_~~





The book exploded in popularity. Months after it came out, _The Boston_
_Morning Post_ said that “everybody has read it, is reading, or is about to read it.”
Throughout the 1800s, the only book that sold more copies than _Uncle Tom’s_
_Cabin_ was the Christian Bible. ~~_Why do you think_~~ ~~Uncle Tom’s Cabin~~ ~~_was so_~~
~~_popular?_~~


Abolitionists worked hard to make sure people could not choose to ignore
the evils of slavery. William Lloyd Garrison was one abolitionist who called
for slavery to be immediately abolished. He believed that the lessons of
the Christian Bible taught that slavery must be ended. Garrison was the
publisher of _The Liberator_ —a famous abolitionist newspaper. _The Liberator_
and other abolitionist newspapers included stories about the evils of slavery.


**Show Image 3A-7: Frederick Douglass**


There were many famous abolitionists, but
perhaps none as well-known as Frederick
Douglass. Douglass was born a slave. When he
was young, he learned to read, and then helped
other slaves read the Christian Bible. After he

and sold copies of books about his life. His
writings and speeches were thoughtful and inspiring, and he became one of
the most famous people in America. Douglass also started his own newspaper
called _The North Star._ He wanted his newspaper to remind people of the
**proverb**, ~~_or a saying passed down from generation to generation,_~~ to “Follow
your North Star.” Just like the North Star in the sky, the newspaper was a guide
for enslaved people escaping slavery and traveling toward freedom in the
North. ~~_Why do you think Frederick Douglass wanted to help other slaves learn_~~
~~_to read?_~~





Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**46**


**Show Image 3A-8: Harriet Tubman**



Harriet Tubman was another famous abolitionist.
She would travel to the South and help enslaved
people escape to freedom in the North, in what
became known as the **Underground Railroad** ~~.~~
~~_The_~~ ~~Underground Railroad~~ ~~_was a network of_~~

~~_enslaved people escape to freedom._~~ It took a lot
of planning and coordination, and she risked her life in the process. Abolitionists
helped keep the Underground Railroad running smoothly, making sure that as
many people as possible were able to escape slavery.





By the year 1850, the United States had expanded to 31 states from the
original 13 colonies. Most states in the North were free; slavery was illegal.
The states in the South allowed slavery, and more people continued to be
enslaved. But the abolitionists would not let the country ignore the issue.
They were determined to make America a land of freedom for everyone.


~~**COMPREHENSION QUESTIONS (10 MIN.)**~~


1. **Literal.** What did the Southern states depend on for their economy?


» The Southern states depended on agriculture or farming—planting

and taking care of crops like cotton, sugar, and tobacco on small farms
and large plantations.


2. **Literal.** What did the Northern states depend on for their economy?


» In the North, the main focus of the economy was industry and

manufacturing. Workers were paid to make things in factories.


3. **Inferential.** Why do you think some people chose to speak out against
slavery, even if it was legal in some parts of the country?


» People like Harriet Beecher Stowe and Frederick Douglass spoke out

against slavery because they thought it was wrong. Their values and
religious beliefs told them that slavery should be illegal.


4. **Inferential.** _Think-Pair-Share:_ Harriet Beecher Stowe, William Lloyd
Garrison, and Frederick Douglass were abolitionists. What did abolitionists
believe about slavery?


» They thought slavery was wrong and wanted it to end. Abolitionists
believed that everyone should be free; they didn’t like slavery because it
was unfair, and they believed that all people should be treated equally.

~~**ELPS 1.E; ELPS 3.D**~~



5. **Inferential.** What influenced their beliefs?


» They were influenced by religion and by what they saw; seeing how

badly slaves were treated, they wanted to make it stop. They read
books and stories that made them think slavery was wrong. Some of
them, like Frederick Douglass, had been slaves themselves, and this
made them want to change things. Religious movements like the Great
Awakenings made them think it was important to treat everyone fairly.


Check for Understanding


**True and False:** [Label two sides of the room or designated areas
“True” and “False.”] The abolitionists had strong beliefs about slavery,
and today we explored those beliefs and what influenced them. I will
read you several statements. Move to the “True” side of the room if
the statement correctly describes a belief or an influence. Move to the
“False” side of the room if the statement does not describe a belief or an
influence.


       - Abolitionists believed that slavery was wrong and should be stopped.

_(True)_


        - Abolitionists, like Frederick Douglass, held the belief that teaching

slaves to read was not a good thing. _(False)_


        - Abolitionists believed that slavery was only fair in the South. _(False)_


       - Abolitionists believed in the importance of freedom for all people.

_(True)_


       - Abolitionists were influenced to keep slavery by conversations they had
with Southern plantation owners. _(False)_


       - Abolitionists were influenced to end slavery by what they read in

popular books like _Uncle Tom’s Cabin_ . _(True)_


       - Abolitionists were influenced to speak out against slavery by the First

and Second Great Awakenings. _(True)_

Lesson 4 Harriet Tubman
**53**


~~**ADVANCE PREPARATION**~~


**Introducing the Read-Aloud**


                 - Use completed Activity Page 3.1 to review what was learned in the previous
Read-Aloud.


**Read-Aloud**


                 - Prepare to display and reference the U.S. Map during the reading or use an
alternate U.S. map for support.


                 - Prepare to distribute Image Card 5, and reference it when described in the
Read-Aloud.


**Application**


                 - Prepare and display the Life and Contributions T-Chart. Alternatively, you
may access a digital version in the online materials for this unit.


**Note to Teacher**


This unit discusses slavery, and this lesson helps students understand what it
was like when enslaved people tried to escape. This lesson focuses on Harriet
Tubman and the Underground Railroad. Students should also know that trying
to escape slavery was arduous, and the Underground Railroad was important
for helping enslaved people on their journey. Emphasize the resilience,
bravery, and sacrifices made by individuals like Harriet Tubman, and explain
the risks she took to lead enslaved people to a better life and to make our
country better.


**Universal Access**


**Introducing the Read-Aloud**


                 - During the Check for Understanding activity, assign students to small groups

and provide options for how they will demonstrate their understanding.
Encourage students to demonstrate by sitting down/standing up, holding
up signs for North/South, or other verbal or nonverbal means as needed.
As they engage, provide students opportunities to discuss within their group
before demonstrating their response.


**Read-Aloud**


                 - During the Read-Aloud, provide additional visual aids or descriptions of key
elements in the story as needed. For example, in addition to the Image Card
and Flip Book images, consider showing pictures of a swamp, a creek, and
a farmhouse to support students with building mental images as they listen.


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**54**


**Application**


  - As students complete Activity Page 4.1, provide sentence frames like the
ones below to support their thinking and writing development on the topic
of Harriet Tubman’s life and contributions. Each frame includes possible
student responses.


    - Harriet Tubman was enslaved and grew up on a _[plantation]_ in _[Maryland]_ .


    - Harriet Tubman escaped to the _[North]_ in search of _[freedom]_ .


    - Harriet Tubman became a conductor on the _[Underground Railroad]_
to help other _[slaves]_ escape.


    - Harriet Tubman was brave and important because she _[helped many_
_slaves escape to freedom]._


~~**CORE VOCABULARY**~~


**conductor, n.** a person who is a guide in charge and often helps passengers
on a railroad, or helped slaves escape to freedom on the Underground
Railroad

Example: Harriet Tubman was a famous _conductor_ on the Underground
Railroad.
Variation(s): conductors


**contributions, n.** money, materials, information, or work given by someone
to help others

Example: Every year, my parents make several _contributions_ to the
American Red Cross to help people in need.
Variation(s): contribution


**gourd, n.** a plant with a hard-shelled fruit that is sometimes dried and
hollowed out to be used as a tool

Example: Pumpkins are a type of _gourd_ .
Variation(s): gourds


**passengers, n.** people who travel in a vehicle like a railroad, and the slaves
seeking freedom on the Underground Railroad

Example: Many of the _passengers_ on the Underground Railroad were caught
before they could reach freedom.
Variation(s): passenger

for runaways miles away from the nearest house, town, or road. The catchers
were paid a lot of money if they caught anyone, and the conductor knew very
well that the catchers would never give up. The conductor who stood guard
over everyone else was Harriet Tubman. ~~_Why do you think enslaved people_~~
~~_would choose to run away?_~~


**Show Image 4A-2: Flashback of Harriet**
**Growing Strong**


Harriet Tubman grew up in Maryland as an
enslaved person on a plantation. ~~[Display~~
~~U.S. Map, and point to Maryland.]~~ Even at the
young age of six years old, she was forced
to work for no pay all day, every day, for the
plantation owner. Enslaved people like her had
no rights or freedom, and Harriet thought she would be enslaved her entire
life. This is what life was like for millions of slaves around the world when
Harriet was young and for many people, long before Harriet or her parents
and grandparents were born.


When the plantation owner died, Harriet Tubman faced a new danger. It
was likely that she would be sent off to Georgia in the deep South. ~~[Point to~~
~~Georgia on the U.S. Map.]~~ This is where many of the plantations grew cotton
and conditions were even worse for enslaved people. Work on the cotton
plantations was difficult and performed in all types of weather. Harriet knew
she had to run away from her life in slavery.


**Show Image 4A-3: Underground Railroad**


To reach freedom in the North, Harriet
Tubman needed to use the Underground
Railroad. ~~_We read about the Underground_~~
~~_Railroad in the last lesson._~~ Remember, this
was not a real railroad, but a system of secret
routes and hiding places to help people
escape slavery and travel to freedom. ~~_What_~~
~~_do you see in this picture? Do you see a train or a railroad? The arrows in the_~~
~~_picture help show how enslaved people escaped from slavery in the South to_~~
~~_freedom in the North._~~ In certain ways, however, the Underground Railroad
was a lot like an actual railroad.


**Passengers** are often people who journey from one place to another in a
vehicle like a train. The runaway slaves on the Underground Railroad were
also known as passengers. ~~_They were slaves who traveled to freedom_~~ . As
soon as runaways got away from the plantation, they were on an incredible


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**60**


and difficult journey to freedom, and they could not complete this journey
without help from a conductor. On a real railroad, a conductor is in charge of
the train, but on the Underground Railroad, a conductor guided runaways.
The conductor led them through secret paths and took them to safe houses.
These safe houses were known as stations. Like real train stations, they
were places where passengers could rest before moving on to the next part
of their journey. Many different people from both the North and the South,
who knew slavery was wrong, provided these stations to those who escaped.
~~_What did a conductor on the Underground Railroad do? What were the_~~
~~_enslaved people on the Underground Railroad called? Where did they rest?_~~


**Show Image 4A-4: William Lloyd Garrison and**
**Frederick Douglass**


In the last lesson, we talked about how there
were thousands of abolitionists. ~~_Remember,_~~
~~_abolitionists were people who worked to_~~
~~_abolish, or end, slavery._~~ We learned about
William Lloyd Garrison and Frederick
Douglass, who worked to help slaves achieve
freedom. Frederick Douglass published a newspaper called _The North_
_Star_ after the star in the sky that would guide enslaved people to their
freedom. Harriet Tubman made it safely to freedom in the North using the
Underground Railroad with the North Star to guide her.


**Show Image 4A-5: Harriet Tubman**


Enslaved people like Harriet were free in
northern states like Pennsylvania, where
slavery was not allowed, but Harriet did not
stay in Pennsylvania for long. She missed
her family and friends and could not bear the
thought of them remaining in slavery while
she enjoyed a free, new life. She decided she
had important contributions to make to help those who were still enslaved,
so she became a conductor on the Underground Railroad. ~~_Contributions_~~
~~_can be money, materials, information, or work given by someone to help_~~
~~_others._~~ She returned to the South 19 more times over several years. Each
time, she risked her life to help other enslaved people escape to freedom.
Harriet Tubman was one of the bravest and most famous conductors of the
Underground Railroad. Her name became well known among the supporters
of slavery, and plantation owners put rich rewards out for her capture. Within
a few years, they wanted her stopped at all costs. Even so, she kept going
back, again and again, helping more and more enslaved people escape.



Challenge


Explain the risks an
enslaved person took
to return to the South
after gaining freedom
in the North. Ask: Why
would someone do that?
Have students identify
characteristics that
describe Harriet Tubman
based on her actions.



Lesson 4 Harriet Tubman
**61**


**Show Image 4A-6: Tubman Leading People**
**Out of Swamp**


Now let’s read from a story that imagines
what it would be like for Harriet and those
with her on one of her trips conducting the
Underground Railroad while leading enslaved
people to freedom.


It was nighttime in 1856. Harriet was startled
by the distant sound of strangers yelling. She knew danger was near.


“Wake up, now. Wake up!” she urged, shaking the men and women. “Gather
up these babies. We’ve got to get a move on.”


The men and women sprang to their feet with fear and panic.


“Don’t you worry now,” she assured them. “I know a station not too far from
here, but we’ll have to move fast, and we’ll have to stay in the creek to keep
the men off our trail.” They hurried out of the swamp and splashed up the
creek. An hour later, soaked in sweat and muddy creek water, they arrived in
the front yard of a small farmhouse. ~~_What was a station on the Underground_~~
~~_Railroad?_~~


**Show Image 4A-7: Woman Helping the**
**Fugitives**


The runaway slaves hid in the weeds while
Harriet Tubman slipped through the yard
and onto the front porch. She tapped three
times on the door, waited a moment, and then
tapped two more times. ~~[Demonstrate this~~
~~secret knock.]~~ This was a secret knock. The
people in the house would know their visitor was an Underground Railroad
conductor in need of help. ~~[~~ ~~_Why do you think they needed a secret knock?]_~~


After hearing the secret knock, a white woman opened the door. The woman
signaled for the runaways to follow her into the chicken coop ~~_(a place where_~~
~~_chickens live)_~~ . There, she lifted a trapdoor in the floor, revealing a dark hole.


“It’s not comfortable,” she told them, “but nobody will find you here. I have
some stew and biscuits inside, and fresh milk. I’ll bring it out as soon as the
coast is clear.”


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**62**


Their hearts raced as they waited in their hideout. ~~_Why were their hearts_~~
~~_racing? How do you think they were feeling?_~~ Running through the creek had
thrown the catchers off the trail, and for now, the runaways were safe. They
wanted to sleep, but when the sun went down, they had to move on again.
There was no time to waste because nighttime was the only safe time to
travel. ~~_Why do you think nighttime was the safest time to travel?_~~


**Show Image 4A-8: Tubman Pointing Out the**
**“Drinking Gourd”**


Outside, Harriet Tubman looked up to the
starry sky. She put her arm around one of
the children. “See there?” she said, pointing
upward. “That group of stars up there . . . It
kind of looks like what you might use to scoop
water from a bucket. Back where I’m from, we
used to call it a drinking **gourd** . ~~[Show Image Card 5 (Hollowed-out Gourd).]~~
~~_A_~~ ~~gourd~~ ~~_is a plant with a hard-shelled fruit that is sometimes dried and_~~
~~_hollowed out to be used as a tool._~~ Do you see it?”


“I think so,” the child said.


“Now, see the ‘cup’ of that drinking gourd?” Harriet Tubman asked. “Look at
the two stars at the end of the cup and pretend you can draw a straight line
from those two stars, straight out into space. If you follow that line straight
out, you will find the North Star. It is always there, right in the same spot, and
you can always find it if you know how to find the drinking gourd in the sky.
Do you know why that star is so important?”


“Why?” the child asked.


“Because the North Star is always to the north and one of the brightest in
the sky. If we follow the North Star every night and keep it in front of us, then
it will guide us north to freedom.”


And heading North to freedom is exactly what they did.

Lesson 4 Harriet Tubman
**63**


~~**COMPREHENSION QUESTIONS (10 MIN.)**~~


1. **Literal.** Why did Harriet Tubman decide to fight back rather than accept her
life as an enslaved person?


» Harriet Tubman was afraid she would be sent off to the deep South.
This is where many of the plantations grew cotton and conditions were
even worse for enslaved people. She ran away because she thought she
could make it to the North.


2. **Literal.** What was the Underground Railroad?


» It was a secret path of routes and hiding places to help enslaved people
escape from slavery in the South to freedom in the North.


3. **Literal.** Was the Underground Railroad a real railroad?


» No.


4. **Literal.** Who were the passengers on the Underground Railroad?


» Slaves trying to escape


5. **Literal.** Name one conductor of the Underground Railroad.


» Harriet Tubman


6. **Literal.** What were Harriet Tubman’s contributions to help enslaved people
escape slavery and win their freedom?


» She became a conductor on the Underground Railroad; she went back
and helped her family and many other enslaved people escape their
unfair lives in slavery.


7. **Inferential.** Why did Harriet Tubman choose to be a conductor on the
Underground Railroad and risk her own life to help other enslaved people?


» She wanted them to be free like she was.


8. **Evaluative.** _Think-Pair-Share:_ What do you think about the actions Harriet
Tubman took to help enslaved people gain their freedom? Use details from
the story to explain your answer.


» Answers may vary but should include an understanding of the
following: Harriet Tubman was kind to help people escape even when
it was dangerous. She was like a hero guiding people through the
Underground Railroad in the dark with people chasing them. Harriet
Tubman was brave and it must have been hard for her because she had
to be careful with catchers. She was smart to remember the way and to
use the North Star as a guide in the dark.


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**64**


Check for Understanding


**Show and Hide:** I am going to read you some statements. If the
statement I read you correctly describes Harriet Tubman and the
Underground Railroad, show me by opening your hands out in front of
you. If the statement I read does not describe Harriet Tubman and the
Underground Railroad, hide your hands behind your back. Let’s see how
well you remember the story we just talked about!

       - Harriet Tubman risked her life to help other enslaved people escape to

freedom. (Show hands.)


       - The Underground Railroad was a real train system. (Hide hands.)


       - Runaway slaves hid while Harriet Tubman knocked on the door with a

secret pattern. (Show hands.)


       - The Underground Railroad operated only during the daytime. (Hide

hands.)


       - The North Star was used as a guide for enslaved people traveling on the

Underground Railroad. (Show hands.)


~~**WORD WORK: CONTRIBUTIONS (5 MIN.)**~~


1. In the Read-Aloud, you heard, “She decided she had important contributions
to make to help those who were still enslaved, so she became a conductor on
the Underground Railroad.”


2. Say the word _contributions_ with me.


3. A contribution can be money, materials, information, or work given by
someone to help others.


4. The kids at Lakewood Elementary School gave food and blankets to
families who didn’t have homes, and everyone was very thankful for their
_contributions_ .


5. Have you ever given a contribution? Share about a time when you gave a
contribution or a time when you accepted a contribution. Try to use the word
_contribution_ when you talk about it. [Ask two or three students. If necessary,
guide and/or rephrase students’ responses: “I gave a contribution to_____.
or “I got a contribution from_____.]


6. What’s the word we’ve been talking about?

**Lesson 4: Harriet Tubman**
#### Application


**Writing:** Students will write an entry in a Civil War journal to describe Harriet

Tubman’s life and contributions.


~~**TEKS 2.7.B; TEKS 2.7.C; TEKS 2.7.E**~~


~~**LIFE AND CONTRIBUTIONS T-CHART (5 MIN.)**~~


  - Display the Life and Contributions T-chart you prepared earlier.


  - Explain that students are going to share what they have learned about the
life and contributions of Harriet Tubman. Tell them that you will record their
responses in the corresponding columns on the T-chart.


Think-Pair-Share


  - Assign students to pairs to talk about what they have learned about Harriet
Tubman’s life as an enslaved person. Ask the following questions to guide the
activity:


  - Where did Harriet Tubman grow up?


» Maryland


  - What challenges did she face?


» She was an enslaved person forced to work for no pay at the age of 6.


  - Why did Harriet Tubman decide to run away?


» The plantation owner died so she was afraid she would be sent off to
Georgia in the deep South where many of the plantations grew cotton
and conditions were even worse for enslaved people.


  - As students share aloud, record responses in the corresponding column on
the T-chart.


  - Next, have student pairs share what they have learned about Harriet
Tubman’s contributions to slavery as a free person. Ask the following
questions to guide the activity:


    - What were Harriet Tubman’s contributions to end slavery?


» She was part of the Underground Railroad; she went back to the South
to help enslaved people escape to freedom.


**TEKS 2.7.B** Write brief comments on literary or informational texts that demonstrate an understanding of the text; **TEKS**
**2.7.C** Use text evidence to support an appropriate response; **TEKS 2.7.E** Interact with sources in meaningful ways such as
illustrating or writing.

Students will review how Harriet Tubman took action to fight back against

the phrase “a house divided.”

Bells” to make connections to “a house divided.”
