Lesson 5 A House Divided
**71**


~~**ADVANCE PREPARATION**~~


**Read-Aloud**


                  - Prepare to display and refer to the U.S. Map, which can be found in the Digital

Components for this unit. This map of the United States will remain on

display throughout the lesson.


**Application**


                 - Prepare to display the poem “Christmas Bells,” which can be found in the

Digital Components for this unit.


**Universal Access**


**Read-Aloud**


                 - Assign small groups to discuss their learning about the phrase “a house

divided” before beginning the Check for Understanding activity. Possible

discussion questions include the following:


                          - How is America like a house?


                          - Why was America divided before and during the Civil War?


**Application**


                 - Prepare and provide printed lists of the prompts prior to the Sayings and
Phrases activity to allow for visual support and increased processing time.


~~**CORE VOCABULARY**~~


**avid, adj.** eager; having a strong interest or desire
Example: Charlie is an _avid_ swimmer; she looks forward to her swim meets.
Variation(s): none


**Congress, n.** elected officials who make important decisions for the country
Example: The _Congress_ met today to vote on a new rule.
Variation(s): Congressional


**elected, adj.** chosen by a vote to do something or to serve in a certain
position
Example: Eugenia was _elected_ by her fellow students to be the new class
president.
Variation(s): elect, elects, electing


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**72**


**endure, v.** to last over time
Example: The tent my grandpa gave me was able to _endure_ even through a
long winter of snow.
Variation(s): enduring


**humble, adj.** respectful; avoiding thinking you are better than others
Example: Pedro was _humble_ ; after winning the race, he never bragged about
his victory to his friends or family.
Variation(s): humbling


**integrity, n.** honest and sincere
Example: Mark showed _integrity_ by telling the truth even though it was hard.
Variation(s): none


**Mason-Dixon Line, n.** an invisible line between Maryland and Pennsylvania
that separated free states and slave states
Example: The enslaved people escaped to the other side of the _Mason-_
_Dixon Line_ .
Variation(s): none


**rebel, v.** to go against leadership
Example: John decided to _rebel_ against the rules during bedtime.
Variation(s): rebellion


**tense, adj.** causing a nervous or suspenseful feeling
Example: Marie felt _tense_ when she was running late for the school bus.
Variation(s): none


**territories, n.** areas of land under control of the government, but not yet
states
Example: Before they became states, large areas of land in the West were
known as _territories._
Variation(s): territory

**Recall:** [Prompt a discussion about Harriet Tubman with the following
questions:]


        - How did Harriet Tubman contribute to freeing enslaved people?


» She became a conductor on the Underground Railroad; she went

back and helped her family and many other enslaved people

escape their unfair lives in slavery.


        - How did the North Star help Harriet Tubman with her mission?


» The North Star was the guide that helped Harriet Tubman lead

enslaved people to freedom in the North.


        - What was Harriet Tubman’s life like as a young child?


» She was an enslaved person, working on a plantation in Maryland.

She had to use the Underground Railroad to escape when the

plantation owner tried to send her to another plantation in

Georgia.


        - How would you describe Harriet Tubman?


» Answers may vary. Students could say she is brave, strong, and/

or courageous.

Support

~~**ESSENTIAL BACKGROUND INFORMATION OR TERMS (5 MIN.)**~~


 - Explain that today’s Read-Aloud describes the conflict between the North
and South leading up to the Civil War.


 - Read the title of the Read-Aloud to students.


 - Explain that the term “a house divided” is a phrase that has a deeper
meaning than what it sounds like.


 - Explain that in today’s Read-Aloud, they will learn about how the country was
divided by an imaginary line between slave states and free states.


 - Ask students why they think there was a conflict about slavery.


 - Remind students that abolitionists were people who fought to end slavery.


 - Explain that Abraham Lincoln was the president of the United States at that
time, and he believed slavery would need to end for the country to be united.

Challenge

~~_This land included the state of Louisiana._~~ At first, all of that land was
organized as territories. Territories had a small number of people and started
to set up small governments of their own until they were organized enough
to become states.


Each time a territory wanted to become a state, **Congress** had to vote to
admit the state into the Union. In the first half of the 1800s, this came with
a big question. Would the state allow people to be slaves, or would it make
slavery illegal? In other words, would it be a slave state or a free state?


This only made the disagreements about slavery worse, because each time
a state was admitted into the national government compromises needed
to be made. There were **tense** debates, or ~~_debates that caused feelings of_~~
~~_nervousness and suspense_~~, between those who wanted to keep slavery and
those who wanted to abolish it. ~~_Why do you think the debates were_~~ ~~tense~~ ~~_or_~~
~~_stressful?_~~ The Founding Fathers, who thought slavery would go away on its
own, realized the problem was only getting worse. Former President Thomas
Jefferson wrote to a friend in 1820 about one of the early compromises made
in Congress to admit free and slave states at the same time: “[the] question,
like a fire bell in the night, awakened and filled me with terror. I considered
it at once as the [death] knell of the Union.” ~~_A knell is the sound a bell_~~
~~_makes when it rings. In this case, President Jefferson was worried that this_~~
~~_compromise to keep slavery was like a bell signaling that the United States_~~
~~_might stop being a country._~~ Jefferson went on to write, “This is a reprieve
only, not a final sentence.” ~~_A reprieve happens when you get a temporary_~~
~~_or short relief from punishment. In this case, President Jefferson noted that_~~
~~_compromise was only a temporary way of dealing with the question of slavery,_~~
~~_but that the problem of slavery would still need to be addressed._~~



Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**78**


Dozens of states joined the Union in the first half of the 1800s, and each
time, the question of whether it would be a free state or a slave state had
to be answered. Let’s go back to the year 1850, when Harriet Tubman
escaped from a life of slavery in the South by running away to Pennsylvania,
a Northern state where slavery was not allowed. ~~[Display the U.S. Map and~~
~~point to the state of Pennsylvania.]~~ To divide the North and the South on
a map of the United States, it is easiest if you use what is known as the
**Mason-Dixon Line.** The Mason-Dixon Line is an imaginary line between the
border of Pennsylvania and Maryland. ~~[Point to the Mason-Dixon Line on~~
~~the map.]~~ It was named after two Englishmen, Charles Mason and Jeremiah
Dixon, who surveyed ~~_or looked carefully at_~~ land almost 100 years earlier.
The Mason-Dixon Line became an imaginary line between the North and the
South. ~~[Refer again to the Mason-Dixon Line on the map.] Were~~ ~~_the slave_~~
~~_states above or below the_~~ ~~Mason-Dixon line~~ ~~_?_~~


Slavery was generally allowed in the South, below the Mason-Dixon Line, but
slavery was not generally allowed in the North, above the Mason-Dixon Line.

~~[Identify and point to some of the states north and south of the Mason-Dixon~~
~~line on the Image 5A-2.]~~ But this was not automatic ~~_or already decided._~~ Each
time a state was allowed to join the Union, the question had to be decided,
which meant more and more arguments over slavery. Sometimes, those
arguments became violent.


~~[Point to the unorganized territory above Texas.] C~~ onsider ~~_or think about_~~
what happened in Kansas. Today, Kansas is a state, but in the 1850s, Kansas
was still a territory. When it was considering whether to become a state, it
needed to decide whether slavery was going to be permitted or outlawed.
People from all over the United States, from the North and the South, from
free states and slave states, traveled to Kansas, sometimes only to have a
chance to influence that decision. Some of them got into fights, and some
people even died.

**Show Image 5A-3: Enslaved Woman**
**Protecting Children**







**Show Image 5A-4: Abraham Lincoln’s**
**Early Home**

During these years of growing tension over slavery, a **humble**, ~~_or respectful_~~, leader emerged
from the backwoods of what was then the
American West. His name was Abraham Lincoln.



Lincoln was born in a one-room log cabin in
1809 in Kentucky. He was born very poor but learned the value of hard work
at a young age. Lincoln’s parents could not read or write. He spent little time
in local schools because he had to work to help support his family. Instead
of going to school to learn, Lincoln taught himself by reading books that he
borrowed. When he was not working on his family’s farm, he loved to learn and
was an **avid** reader. He read and re-read the Christian Bible throughout his
life. ~~_So far, what are some things we have heard about Abraham Lincoln?_~~


Lincoln had many jobs. He was a shopkeeper and a postmaster, and he
worked on a small riverboat. ~~_(Postmasters helped to deliver mail.)_~~ He taught


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**80**


himself law and became a lawyer in 1836. Ten years later, Lincoln was
**elected** as a Congressman in the U.S. House of Representatives. Abraham
Lincoln was a man of such **integrity** that he earned the nickname “Honest
Abe.” ~~_What words in that sentence help us know what_~~ ~~integrity~~ ~~_means?_~~


Abraham Lincoln spent much of his life living in the Northern, free states. He
was firmly against slavery’s expansion.


In 1858, years before Lincoln would run for President, Lincoln became the
Republican candidate running to become a U.S. Senator for the state of
Illinois. The country was on edge over whether slavery would expand to new
states. ~~_What might it mean to be on edge?_~~



**Show Image 5A-5: President Lincoln**
Speaking at the 1858 Illinois Republican State
Convention, Lincoln gave a historic campaign
speech. He argued that there should be no
compromise on the issue of whether to allow
slavery in the United States. Our nation was

supported slavery. There was no in-between.
Lincoln was a man with deeply held Christian beliefs, and like abolitionists
throughout the country, Lincoln made it clear that he was on the side of
freedom to keep the country united. ~~_Based on Lincoln’s speech, if he won_~~
~~_the presidency, do you think he would fight to keep slavery or free the slaves?_~~
~~_How do you know?_~~





Lincoln declared, “‘A house divided against itself cannot stand.’ I believe this
government cannot **endure**, permanently, half slave and half free. I do not
expect the Union to be dissolved; I do not expect the house to fall; but I do
expect it will cease to be divided. It will become all one thing, or all the other.”
Lincoln’s “house divided” was a quote spoken by Jesus in the Christian Bible
and was widely understood by Americans at that time. The Bible quotes
Jesus as saying: “Every kingdom divided against itself will be ruined, and
every city or household divided against itself will not stand.” ~~_What did Lincoln_~~
~~_mean by “it will become all one thing, or all the other?”_~~

from the Christian Bible,

talking about an actual
house or something else?

Lesson 5 A House Divided
**81**


Support


Demonstrate for students
the concepts of divided and
united using your hands.
Ask students to place
the palms of their hands

together. Now pull them
apart. Say: This is divided

or separated. Now place
your hands back together.

Say: This is united, which

means together. Lincoln
wanted the country to be

united, or stay together.



Lincoln was one of two politicians running in
the 1858 election for the U.S. Senate in Illinois.
~~_(Politicians are part of a government, which_~~
~~_is a group of people who help lead a country.)_~~

named Stephen Douglas. Lincoln did not want
to see slavery expanded into any new states, and he thought that Congress
should make laws to prevent it from expanding. Douglas thought the question of slavery should be answered by each state, and the power of Congress
should be limited. The two politicians participated in many debates, and both
men made intelligent, ~~_or smart_~~, arguments. Their arguments were written
down and published in newspapers and books. These Lincoln-Douglas debates became famous, with people all over America following the two politicians and their ideas.



**Show Image: 5A-6: Lincoln Standing to**
**Debate Douglas**





Lincoln lost that year’s Illinois Senate election to the Democrat Stephen
Douglas, but the anti-slavery arguments he made in the Lincoln-Douglas debates skyrocketed him to national fame. ~~_This means that many people knew_~~
~~_him and believed in him._~~ He became the leading figure of the Republican party. The Republican party had been newly formed in the United States, founded on the goal of abolition. ~~_Do you remember what abolition means?_~~ Two
years later, Lincoln was the Republican nominee for President. No state in
the South voted for him. But voters from every free state in the North chose
to elect Lincoln as President, and that was enough for him to win. He became
the 16th President of the United States in 1861. Those supporting abolition
looked to Lincoln to help negotiate an end to slavery in the country. ~~Negoti-~~
~~ate~~ ~~_means to come to an agreement in a respectful way so that both sides can_~~
~~_live with the results._~~ Yet those in the South could see no compromise with
Lincoln. Many people in the South believed that President Lincoln wanted to
take away their right as a state to set their own laws and, specifically, that
he wanted to abolish slavery. The reprieve from conflict on the question of
slavery was about to come to an end. The Civil War would soon begin.


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**82**


~~**COMPREHENSION QUESTIONS (10 MIN.)**~~


1. **Inferential.** What did Abraham Lincoln believe about slavery?


» He believed slavery needed to end. He felt ending slavery was important
to moving the country forward in a good way.


2. **Evaluative.** Do you believe America is stronger united or divided? Why?


» Answers may vary but should indicate that being united makes us
stronger.


3. **Evaluative.** Why do you think Lincoln thought there was no in-between—that
the United States was either a nation that supported slavery or one that
stood for freedom?


» Answers may vary but should indicate that it would be impossible for a
country founded on a belief that all men are created equal to treat some
as slaves.


Check for Understanding


**Turn and Talk:** With your partner, discuss how you think President
Lincoln felt about slavery and where his views came from.


[Have several students share their responses with the class.]


» Northern “free states” believed slavery should be illegal because
it is wrong. Lincoln agreed with them for several reasons. He grew
up in a free state. He also felt slavery was dividing the country, so
ending it was important to unify the country.

**Lesson 5: A House Divided**

#### Application


**Language:** Students will discuss the use of descriptive and figurative

language, including the phrase “a house divided.”

~~**TEKS 2.3.D; TEKS 2.10.D**~~

**Writing:** Students will complete a poetry analysis activity using the poem

~~**TEKS 2.7.B; TEKS 2.7.E; TEKS 2.10.D**~~


~~**SAYINGS AND PHRASES “A HOUSE DIVIDED” (5 MIN.)**~~


  - Explain to students that a metaphor is a way of explaining things by
comparing two things that are not the same.


  - Metaphors usually are used to help readers understand by helping to create
pictures in your mind.


  - Ask students if they have ever heard the saying “a house divided” before
today’s Read-Aloud.


  - Have students repeat the saying.


  - Explain to students that _divided_ means split apart.


  - Ask students what pictures they see in their mind when thinking about a
house divided.


  - Explain to students “a house divided” is not really a house. Lincoln was
comparing America to a big house because all of the states are connected by
one land just like a house is under one roof.


  - Explain that the phrase “a house divided” is from a quote by Jesus in the
Christian Bible. Lincoln used the quote to show how he felt about America
having free states and slave states instead of a strong union of states.


  - Ask students if they ever had a conflict with someone in their family that

needed to be fixed. Give students the opportunity to share their experiences,
and encourage them to use the saying.


**TEKS 2.3.D** Identify, use, and explain the meaning of antonyms, synonyms, idioms, and homographs in context; **TEKS 2.10.D**
Discuss the use of descriptive, literal, and figurative language; **TEKS 2.7.B** Write brief comments on literary or informational
texts that demonstrate an understanding of the text; **TEKS 2.7.E** Interact with sources in meaningful ways such as illustrating
or writing.

Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**86**


**Grade 2 | Unit 7**
#### Pausing Point 1


~~**NOTE TO TEACHER**~~


You should pause here and spend two days reviewing, reinforcing, or
extending the material taught so far.


You may have students do any combination of the activities listed below,
but it is highly recommended that you use the Mid-Unit Assessment to
assess students’ knowledge of what slavery is and where it was practiced
historically, the Great Awakenings, the abolitionists and Harriet Tubman,
the Northern and Southern economies, and the events and primary issues
leading to the U.S. Civil War. The other activities may be done in any order.
You may also choose to do an activity with the whole class or with a small
group of students who would benefit from the particular activity.


~~**CORE CONTENT OBJECTIVES UP TO THIS PAUSING POINT**~~


Students will:


  - Describe what slavery is and identify examples of it throughout global
history.


  - Explain what the Great Awakenings were and what many of the Founding
Fathers came to believe about slavery.


  - Describe what the abolitionists believed about slavery and what influenced
them.


  - Identify the Underground Railroad as a system of escape for enslaved people
in the United States.


  - Describe the life and contributions of Harriet Tubman.


  - Identify the differences between the Northern and Southern economies
leading up to the Civil War.


  - Describe Abraham Lincoln’s beliefs about slavery.


Pausing Point 1
**87**


Activity Page PP.1



~~**MID-UNIT ASSESSMENT**~~


**Civil War Match-Up**


Use Activity Page PP.1 to assess students’ knowledge of the content covered
so far in _The U.S. Civil War_ . Read each sentence to students, as well as the
word choices in the word bank, to ensure understanding. Repeat as needed.


1. I am a form of government where people are free to vote for their leaders and

decide what kind of laws they want to have. What am I called?


» democracy


2. I am a very large farm where large amounts of crops are grown and harvested

by enslaved labor. What am I called?


» plantation


3. I am a religious movement in the American colonies during the 1720s-1740s.

What am I called?


» First Great Awakening


4. We are a group of men led by George Washington who helped the colonies

separate from Great Britain to establish the government of the United States.
Who are we?


» Founding Fathers


5. I am someone who worked to end slavery. What am I called?


» abolitionist


6. I am the secret way enslaved Africans people escaped to freedom in the
North. What am I called?


» Underground Railroad


7. My job was to help lead enslaved people, called passengers, to freedom on

the Underground Railroad. What am I called?


» conductor


8. I am an imaginary boundary between the states of Pennsylvania and

Maryland, and I separate where slavery was allowed and where it was not.
What am I called?


» Mason-Dixon Line


9. I am the central issue that caused the U.S. Civil War. What am I?


» slavery


10.I was elected president because people in the North believed I would not

allow slavery to expand. Who am I?



» Abraham Lincoln

Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**88**


~~**ACTIVITIES**~~


**Image Review**

 - Show the Flip Book images from any Read-Aloud again, and have students
retell the Read-Aloud using the images.


**Describing Image Cards**


Materials: Image Cards 1–5


 - Distribute Image Cards 1–5 and have students describe them in small groups.

Challenge students to identify the region associated with the item in the
image (e.g., in the North or South) and who it was used by.


**You Were There: The Underground Railroad, Lincoln-Douglas Debates**

 - Have students pretend that they lived during the time of the U.S. Civil War.


 - Ask students to describe what they saw and heard. For example, students
may talk about meeting Harriet Tubman while escaping on the Underground
Railroad, traveling under the North Star, etc. They may talk about hearing
strangers yelling, seeing secret passages for hiding escaped enslaved people,
etc.


 - Consider also extending this activity by adding small-group or independent
writing opportunities associated with the “You Were There” concept. For
example, ask students to pretend they are newspaper reporters describing
one of the Lincoln-Douglas debates and write a group news article describing
the event.


**Key Vocabulary Brainstorming**


Materials: Chart paper


 - Give students a key unit concept or vocabulary word such as _slavery_ . Have
them brainstorm everything that comes to mind when they hear the word,
such as the South, plantations, etc. Record their responses on chart paper
for reference.


**Class Book: The U.S. Civil War**


Materials: Drawing paper, drawing tools


 - Tell the class or a group of students that they are going to make a class book
to help them remember what they have learned so far in this unit.


 - Have students brainstorm important information about slavery, the Great
Awakenings, the North and the South, abolitionists, Harriet Tubman, and
Abraham Lincoln. Have each student choose one idea to draw a picture of
and ask them to write a caption for the picture.



Flip Book


Image Cards 1–5

Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**90**


**Compare/Contrast**


Materials: Chart paper


 - Tell students that there are many things to compare and contrast in the
Read-Alouds they have heard so far.


 - Remind students that to compare means to tell how things or people are
similar and to contrast means to tell how things or people are different. Have
students choose a topic from the following list to compare/contrast on a
chart. You may do this individually or as a class.


   - the Revolutionary War and the Civil War


   - the North and the South (before the Civil War, including ways of life,
economy, major products, etc.)


   - freedom and slavery


   - Lincoln and Douglas


 - You may wish to extend this activity by using the chart as a prewriting tool
and having students write two paragraphs, one describing similarities and
the other describing differences.


**Cotton**


Materials: Image Card 1; various objects made from cotton


 - Show Image Card 1 (Cotton).


 - Ask students what this plant is called. Ask why cotton was an important crop

to the North and the South. Ask students to recall who gathered the cotton
from the plantation fields ( _slaves_ ).


 - Place various objects on a table (cotton balls, cotton swabs, clothing, yarn,
towels, sheets, pillowcases, etc.) and allow students to look at them and
touch them. Ask students what they think life would be like without cotton.
Tell them that many people all over the world depended on cotton at the time
of the Civil War and still do today. Reiterate that this was why cotton was so
important. Ask students to think of other things that are made from cotton.

Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War

~~**CORE VOCABULARY**~~


**clash, v.** to collide in intense disagreement

Example: Sergio and his younger brother would often _clash_ over who would
get to use the computer first.
Variation(s): clashes, clashed, clashing


**Confederacy, n.** the government formed by the states in the South after
they withdrew from the United States

Example: The _Confederacy_ was prepared to fight to prevent the North from
having total control.
Variation(s): none


**devastated, v.** destroyed

Example: The forest was _devastated_ by the tornado.
Variation(s): devastate, devastates, devastating


**port city, n.** a city on the water with a loading area where ships can bring
and take large amounts of goods

Example: The sailors traveled to the _port city_ to unload cargo from their
ship.
Variation(s): none


**Rebels, n.** the nickname given to the Confederate soldiers

Example: The _Rebels_ became known for their loud battle cry.
Variation(s): rebel


**retreated, v.** went back or moved away from something, usually in defeat

Example: After hearing the loud noise, the scared cat _retreated_ under the
bed to hide.
Variation(s) retreat, retreating


**seceded, v.** withdrew from or stopped being a member of a particular group

Example: People have _seceded_ from groups throughout history, usually
to form a new group.
Variation(s): secede, secedes, seceding


**surrender, v.** give something up or agree to stop fighting and let the other
side win

Example: Anthony would _surrender_ the remote control to his brother.
Variation(s): surrendered, surrenders, surrendering


**unify, v.** bring things or people together to work as a team

Example: The different students worked together to _unify_ their artwork for
the group show.
Variation(s): unified


Lesson 6 The War Begins
**95**


**Union, n.** the Northern states that did not secede from the United States

Example: The _Union_ was prepared to fight to keep the states together.
Variation(s): none

Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**100**


**Show Image 6A-4: Fort Sumter**


Fort Sumter was a United States federal
fort located in South Carolina. After South
Carolina seceded from the Union, federal
soldiers continued to occupy the fort and the
Confederates wanted them to leave because
it was located in Confederate territory. The
states that had seceded viewed themselves
as an independent nation with their own government and an army. The
Confederacy would lose the respect of foreign nations if they allowed the U.S.
to have troops within their territory. The Confederates asked the soldiers to
**surrender**, ~~_or give up,_~~ but they refused because they knew President Lincoln
wanted to maintain the fort as federal property and had made the decision
to send supplies. News of supplies coming pushed the South to respond.
Confederate soldiers fired shots at the fort and the federal soldiers in the early
morning hours of April 12, 1861.


**Show Image 6A-5: Map of All States That**
**Seceded from the Union**


A civil war is a war fought between people of the
same nation. Usually, in a civil war, two or more
groups of citizens in a country strongly believe
in something. They fight each other to get their
way. The war that began with the Battle of Fort
Sumter would later come to be known as the
U.S. Civil War, or the War Between the States. It was an awful time in American
history. ~~_Why was it awful for people in the same country to be at war?_~~


After the Battle of Fort Sumter, more Southern states joined the Confederacy,
including Virginia, Arkansas, Tennessee, and North Carolina, as well as
portions of Kentucky and Missouri. In all, there were 13 Confederate states, all
from the South. ~~[Point to show the new states that joined the Confederacy.]~~


**Show Image 6A-6: Union Flag and**
**Confederate Flag**


The newly formed Confederacy even made
a flag to symbolize its new country. The
Confederate flag pictured at the bottom left
has seven stars to symbolize the first set of
states that seceded from the Union. ~~[Point to~~
~~the flag on the bottom left in the image.]~~ The

and the Confederacy got

Lesson 6 The War Begins
**101**


Support


Review the names of the

two sides involved in the

U.S. Civil War.



flag pictured at the bottom right with 13 stars symbolizes the Confederacy
after the Battle of Fort Sumter when more states joined. ~~[Point to the flag on~~
~~the bottom right.]~~


The remaining states, those that had not seceded, were in the North. These
states were known as the Union. For most of our history, the word _Union_ is
what we refer to as all of our states working together as one. But during the
Civil War, _Union_ only referred to the states that did not secede. States in the
Union continued to fly the traditional flag of the United States, pictured at
the top, which at the time of the Civil War had 34 stars—one for each state,
including the Confederate states. ~~_What message was the Union sending by_~~
~~_flying a flag with 34 stars, including Confederate states?_~~


Both sides—the Union and the Confederacy—built up their armies as quickly as
possible. Throughout the North, people wanted President Lincoln to do whatever
he could to end the war quickly, and most people assumed that the war _would_ end
quickly. After all, the Union had more people, more factories, a larger army, and a
powerful navy. ~~_Name a reason most people thought the war would end quickly._~~


The first true test between the armies of the North and the South came in
July 1861 in the state of Virginia. ~~[Point to Virginia on the U.S. Map.]~~ Virginia
is home to the city of Richmond, which was the capital of the Confederacy at
the time of the Civil War. Today, Richmond is the capital of Virginia. Virginia
also touches Washington, D.C., the capital of the United States. ~~[Point to~~
~~Washington, D.C. on the U.S. Map.]~~ Three months after the Battle of Fort
Sumter, thousands of Union and Confederate soldiers met in Virginia for
the first major battle of the Civil War. ~~_Were the Union soldiers fighting for the_~~
~~_North or the South? What about the Confederate soldiers?_~~


As president, Abraham Lincoln was commander in chief of the U.S. Army,
also called the Union Army. He decided to try to end the war quickly. He sent
his army to destroy the Confederate Army in Virginia and capture its capital,
the city of Richmond. The Union Army moved toward the town of Manassas,
where there was a small river called Bull Run.


**Show Image 6A-7: General McDowell and the**
**Army of the Potomac**


The plan was for the Union Army to defeat
whatever forces the Confederacy tried to put
in its path. Then, they would march on and
attack Richmond. The Union Army had 35,000
soldiers. Up to that day, this was the single
largest army ever formed in America. The



Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**102**


Union did not realize, however, that the Confederate Army, or the **Rebels** as
they were also called, had roughly the same number of soldiers in the area.


That July, the Virginia summer heat was so hot it was sometimes hard
to breathe. Union soldiers, many wearing heavy wool clothing, marched
slowly for two days over rolling farmland and across shallow, muddy creeks.
~~_After hearing that sentence, what pictures can you see in your mind?_~~ The
Confederate soldiers waited for them at Manassas.


By July 21, it was clear that the armies were going to **clash** ~~_or have a big_~~
~~_conflict or disagreement._~~ The Confederates decided to attack.


As the Confederates marched across the field, the Union guns opened fire. It
was hard to see what was happening in the smoke-filled air, but it was clear
that the quick victory the Union and their supporters had hoped for was not
coming to pass ~~_or happening._~~


Instead, amid the firing of cannons and rifles, a new sound rang out. This
sound would come to be known as the Rebel Yell. It was a battle cry, which
the Confederates yelled out as they attacked the Union Army. Despite careful
planning, almost nothing went right for the Union Army.


The Confederate cannons were older and less powerful than the Union’s
cannons, but the Confederate soldiers firing them seemed to have more skill.
~~_How do you think Lincoln and the Union soldiers felt at this point?_~~


**Show Image 6A-8: Stonewall Jackson**


Several of the Confederate commanders
seemed to be more capable than the Union
commanders, as well. One commander,
General Thomas Jackson, earned the nickname
Stonewall Jackson when he and his forces
stood like a stone wall against the Union attack.

~~[Point to the picture of Stonewall Jackson.]~~


**Show Image 6A-9 Union Retreat**


Stonewall Jackson and the rest of the
Confederate Army won the First Battle of
Manassas. By late afternoon, the Union Army
broke apart and **retreated** ~~_or moved back_~~
toward the safety of Washington, D.C. ~~_Which_~~
~~_Army was winning at this time, the Union or the_~~
~~_Confederacy? How do you know?_~~

Lesson 6 The War Begins
**103**


**Show Image 6A-10: Civil War Ruins**


Thousands of Union and Confederate soldiers
were wounded or captured in this battle. Many
did not survive. The landscape around the
battlefield was also **devastated** ~~_or destroyed._~~
Roads, bridges, and entire hillsides lay in ruins.


This was just the first of many, many battles
in the years of the Civil War. After that first
battle, which was called the First Battle of Manassas or the First Battle of
Bull Run, President Lincoln and others in the North realized that this Civil
War would not be easy to win. ~~_What made President Lincoln realize that the_~~
~~_war would not be easy?_~~


The Union realized that they would need a much larger army. More
importantly, they would need to prepare for a much longer war. In fact, within
a year, many people were wondering whether the Union would be able to win
the war at all.


~~**COMPREHENSION QUESTIONS (10 MIN.)**~~


1. **Literal.** Who were the two groups fighting in the Civil War?


» The Union and the Confederacy


2. **Literal.** Who were the rebels?


» The Confederate soldiers


3. **Literal.** Why was the Civil War longer and deadlier than the North thought it

would be?


» The Confederate commanders were very strong; the Confederate

soldiers were very skilled; there were many more Confederate soldiers
than the North thought at first.


4. **Inferential.** Who do you think gave Stonewall Jackson his nickname? Why?


» The other soldiers may have because he and his soldiers stood “like a

stone wall” against the Union Army.


5. **Literal.** _Think-Pair-Share:_ Why do you think the Union thought the war would

end quickly and easily?


» The Union had more people, more factories, a larger army, and a

powerful navy.

Check for Understanding


**Stand Up/Sit Down:** I am going to read you four statements. If the
statement I read you is a true statement about the Civil War, stand up. If
the statement I read about the Civil War is false, sit down.


        - Stonewall Jackson and the Confederate Army won the First Battle of

Manassas. ( _stand up_ )


        - The Confederate flag had more stars than the Union flag. ( _sit down_ )


         - At first, the Union thought the war would end quickly. ( _stand up_ )


         - Lincoln was not going to allow the United States of America to be

broken up into two separate countries. ( _stand up_ )


Exit Pass


**List the Reasons:** Write at least two reasons the author gives in the ReadAloud for the Civil War being longer and deadlier than the North thought it
would be. Make sure to support the reasons with evidence from the text.

~~**WORD WORK: UNION AND CONFEDERACY (5 MIN.)**~~


1. In the Read-Aloud you heard, “The Confederate flag pictured at the bottom

left has seven stars to symbolize the first set of states that seceded from the
Union.”


2. Say the word _Union_ with me.


3. Say the word _Confederacy_ with me.


4. The Union is the group of states that did not secede or break away from the
rest of the country. The Confederacy included states that seceded or chose
to break away from the Union.


5. Say a sentence about the Civil War using the words _Union_ and _Confederacy._

[Ask two or three students. If necessary, guide the responses.]


**Do a sharing activity for follow-up.** Pretend you are a news reporter.
How would you describe the early battles between the Union and the
Confederacy? Remember to use both terms in your response.



Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**106**


**Lesson 6: The War Begins**
20m
#### Application


**Language:** Students will distinguish between different speech registers, based on

the audience. ~~**TEKS 2.1.E**~~

**Speaking and Listening:** Students will discuss the use of descriptive and figurative

language, including the phrase “easier said than done.” ~~**TEKS 2.10.D**~~


~~**SYNTACTIC AWARENESS ACTIVITY (15 MIN.)**~~


**Speech Registers**


  - The purpose of these syntactic activities is to help students understand the
direct connection between grammatical structures and the meaning of text.
These syntactic activities should be used in conjunction with the complex
text presented in the Read-Alouds.


  - Explain that we change the way we are speaking depending on the audience
we are speaking to and the situation we are in. When we are talking to
teachers and other adults, we speak one way, and when we are talking to our
friends, we speak another way.


  - Explain that, for example, when students say hello to the principal in the
morning, they might say, “Good morning, Ms./Mr. _____. How are you today?”
When they say hello to a friend in the morning, they might say, “Hey! What’s
up?”


  - Remind students that they heard in the Read-Aloud, “As president, Abraham
Lincoln was commander in chief of the U.S. Army, also called the Union
Army.”


  - Given that Abraham Lincoln was the president of the United States and
commander in chief of the U.S. Army, ask students if they would speak to
Abraham Lincoln as they would greet the principal or as they would greet
their friend. _(principal)_


**TEKS 2.1.E** Develop social communication such as distinguishing between asking and telling; **TEKS 2.10.D** Discuss the use of
descriptive, literal, and figurative language.

me, Mr. President, could I

Check for Understanding


**Turn and Talk:** I am going to give you five different situations. Work with
your partner to show how you would say “hi” in each situation. _(Answers_
_may vary depending on each student’s experience.)_


        - saying hello to your teacher


        - saying hello to your baby brother


       - saying hello to your great-grandmother


        - saying hello to your friend’s parents


        - saying hello to your friend’s brother or sister


~~**SAYINGS AND PHRASES: “EASIER SAID THAN DONE” (5 MIN.)**~~


 - Explain to students that proverbs are short, traditional sayings that have
been passed along orally from generation to generation.


 - These sayings usually express general truths based on experiences and
observations of everyday life. Whereas some proverbs do have literal
meanings—that is, they mean exactly what they say—many proverbs have a
richer meaning beyond the literal level.


 - Ask students if they have ever heard the saying “easier said than done.” Have
students repeat the saying.


 - Explain that if something is “easier said than done,” it is easier to say how the
task should be completed than it is to actually complete the task.


 - Explain that if someone had said to President Lincoln, “This war should be
over by now,” he may have said, “That is easier said than done.”


 - Ask students if they have ever faced a task that was more difficult to
complete than originally thought. Give students the opportunity to share
their experiences and encourage them to use the saying.


 - Try to find opportunities to use this saying in various situations in the
classroom.

~~**ADVANCE PREPARATION**~~


**Introducing the Read-Aloud**


                 - Prepare to display Flip Book Image 6A-4 to reference during the What Have
We Already Learned? segment.

                 - Prepare to display and refer to “Battle Hymn of the Republic” Lyrics during

the Read-Aloud segment.


**Application**


                 - Create a Civil War timeline that begins with the year 1850 and goes through

1865. The timeline should be large enough to accommodate nine image

cards.


                 - Prepare to distribute and refer to Image Cards 6–9 during the Civil War

timeline segment.


                  - At the beginning of the Poetry Analysis, you will show a video of the United
States Army Field Band’s performance of Battle Hymn of the Republic. It is
available on YouTube and other sources. Find, preview, and prepare to show
the video.


                 - At the end of the Poetry Analysis, you will show a video of the Muppets
humming Battle Hymn of the Republic in an elevator. It is available on
YouTube and other sources. Find, preview, and prepare to show the video.


**Universal Access**


**Introducing the Read-Aloud**


                 - Assign small groups to discuss their learning about the significance of Fort

Sumter before beginning the Check for Understanding activity.


**Read-Aloud**


                 - Provide sentence starters for students to access during their Think-Pair
Share discussions.


                          - Union soldiers were _____.


                          - “His truth is marching on” is positive/helpful because _____.


**Application**


                 - Allow time to review and discuss the Civil War timeline prior to adding a new

event to the timeline.


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**112**


~~**CORE VOCABULARY**~~


**casualties, n.** people who are hurt or killed in war

Example: Sadly, the Civil War had many _casualties_ .
Variation(s): casualty


**cause, n.** an idea or goal people are interested in supporting

Example: Sarah’s _cause_ was to help clean up the playground and make it a
fun place for everyone to play.
Variation(s): causes, causing


**emancipation, n.** the act of releasing or setting free

Example: Abolitionists wanted _emancipation_ for the slaves in the South.
Variation(s): emancipations


**hymn, n.** song of praise, often to God

Example: The whole church sang the _hymn_ together.
Variation(s): hymns, hymning


**sacrifice, v.** to give up personal comfort or satisfaction for the sake of
something else

Example: Emily was willing to _sacrifice_ some playtime to help her mom with
chores around the house.
Variation(s): sacrificed


**treason, n.** disloyalty to someone’s own country

Example: As a spy for the British during the Revolutionary War, Benedict
Arnold committed _treason_ against the United States of America.
Variation(s): none

|---|---|---|

|Vocabulary|emancipation_(emancipación)_<br>hymn_(himno)_<br>treason_(traición)_|casualties<br>cause_(causa)_<br>sacrifce_(sacrifcio)_|
|<br>Multiple-Meaning<br>Vocabulary Words|||
|Sayings and<br>Phrases|||

**Lesson 7: Battle Hymn of the Republic**
#### Read-Aloud



30m



Flip Book Images
7A-1–7A-5



**Reading:** Students will analyze poetic language to describe the costs of the Civil War

and explain how Union soldiers and supporters found inspiration to continue to fight.


~~**TEKS 2.8.A; TEKS 2.10.D**~~

_emancipation._


~~**TEKS 2.3.B**~~


~~**PURPOSE FOR LISTENING**~~


  - Tell students to listen carefully to find out the costs of the Civil War and how
the Union soldiers and supporters found inspiration to continue the war. Be
sure to approach the topic of the war’s horrors with sensitivity.


~~**“BATTLE HYMN OF THE REPUBLIC” (15 MIN.)**~~


**Show Image 7A-1: Battle Destruction**


~~_Look at the picture. What does it show you_~~
~~_about the war?_~~ The Civil War was a time of
great **sacrifice** ~~_or giving up important things_~~
~~_for something else_~~ in American history. Many
people were ready to fight for what they
believed in. Many were even willing to give up
their lives. They believed it was a very
important **cause** ~~_or idea or goal that people_~~
~~_were interested in supporting._~~


By some measures, more American soldiers died during the Civil War
than in all other American wars combined. More than 600,000 Union and
Confederate soldiers lost their lives by the end of the war. Additionally, well
over a million men were wounded or injured in the Civil War.


Americans were no strangers to the horrors and death brought on by war.
During the Revolutionary War, many people were hurt and houses and towns
were damaged. In the War of 1812, there were fights on ships in the water,


**TEKS 2.8.A** Discuss topics and determine theme using text evidence with adult assistance.; **TEKS 2.10.D** Discuss the use of
descriptive, literal, and figurative language; **TEKS 2.3.B** Use context within and beyond a sentence to determine the meaning
of unfamiliar words.



Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**116**


and buildings were burned down on land. However, the Civil War proved to be
far more devastating ~~_or destructive_~~ than any war before it. For example, at
the Battle of Antietam in 1862, there were more than 21,000 **casualties** in a
single day. That means that about one of every six soldiers who took the field
that day at Antietam was either killed or wounded within a few hours.


Advancements in weaponry made the war very dangerous. ~~_Weapons were_~~
~~_made better and were easier to use._~~ And unlike other wars, both sides who
fought were Americans. The Civil War pitted “brother against brother,” and
every casualty was an American soldier.


**Show Image 7A-2: African American Union**
**troops**


The soldiers of the Union Army fought for
many different reasons. Some fought because
they saw joining the fight as their patriotic
duty ~~_or responsibility to the country._~~ They

that the Southern states were rebelling and
they thought what those states were doing was **treason** . Others supported
the idea of emancipation, which means freeing a person from slavery. For
example, Harriet Tubman, the abolitionist you learned about earlier, led a raid
of 150 African American soldiers which ended up freeing 700 slaves. She is
credited as being the first woman to lead US soldiers into battle.



Support


Remind students that
the Civil War was fought
by Americans against
Americans, so when the
author says, “brother
against brother,” in most
cases it is figurative
language that means
American against
American. But there
were battles in which
brothers within the same
family were on opposing
sides of the war and
fought each other in
battle.


Support


Students may seek to
understand what 10
percent means. Explain
to students that for
every 10 Union soldiers,
one of them was Black.





There were also nearly 180,000 Black soldiers who fought for the Union. This
was about 10 percent of the total Union Army. These Black soldiers wanted
to fight for their freedom, and they made many significant contributions
during the war. ~~_Look at the picture. What do you see?_~~


**Show Image 7A-3: Frederick Douglass**


As the great abolitionist Frederick Douglass
said, “[L]et the Black man get upon his person
the brass letters U.S., let him get an eagle
on his button, and a musket ~~_or gun_~~ on his
shoulder and bullets in his pockets, and there
is no power on earth which can deny that
he has earned the right of citizenship in the
United States.” ~~_What was Frederick Douglass’s belief about African Americans_~~
~~_joining the Union Army?_~~


Lesson 7 Battle Hymn of the Republic
**117**


Support


Explain to students that
the soldiers were human
and felt emotions like
you and me. When times
were hard during the
war, the hymn kept them
encouraged to keep
going.



**Show Image 7A-4: Union Soldiers with Their**
**Equipment and a Drum**


All the brave soldiers made enormous sacrifices.
Fighting on the battlefield was dangerous and
could be scary. Even when they weren’t in a
battle, there were still many difficulties, such as
having enough food and warm clothes as they
moved from one battlefield to another. They
took a stand for what was right and to save our new nation, but we must not
forget that these Union soldiers were humans, too. There were times that they
were anxious ~~_or nervous,_~~ homesick, or even bored. There were times when they
were tired, injured, sick, hungry, and cold. They had to find a way to stay focused
on the noble cause of preserving ~~_or saving_~~ the Union and bringing slavery to
an end. One of the ways that the soldiers boosted their morale, or their mood,
when times were tough was through music. ~~_Why do you think it was important_~~
~~_for soldiers to be in a better mood?_~~


**Show Image 7A-5: Julia Ward Howe**


In November 1861, poet and abolitionist Julia
Ward Howe went to Washington, D.C. to visit
Union troops. Howe was with her minister who
encouraged her to write a song to inspire the
soldiers, and that she did! She wrote a hymn,
or a song of praise to God, that became known
as “Battle Hymn of the Republic.” ~~[Display~~
~~“Battle Hymn of the Republic” Lyrics.]~~


The “Battle Hymn of the Republic” became very popular among Union
troops. It also became popular with all their supporters at home. There were
many families throughout the North whose fathers, brothers, and sons were
fighting in the war, and the song was a reminder of why the war needed to be
fought. ~~_Why did the Civil War need to be fought?_~~ Throughout history, people
have turned to poetry and music to inspire them to work through difficulties
toward important causes. This hymn reminded the people and soldiers of the
Union that they were fighting for a just ~~_or morally right_~~ cause. Long after the
war was over, the lyrics of the song have been quoted by many in America
who are fighting to support freedom and justice. ~~_How do poetry and music_~~
~~_help people?_~~



Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**118**


~~**COMPREHENSION QUESTIONS (10 MIN.)**~~


1. **Literal.** What sacrifices did Union soldiers make during the Civil War?


» There were times when they were tired, injured, sick, hungry, and cold.

There were times that they were anxious [or nervous], homesick, or
even bored. Many were injured or killed.


2. **Inferential.** Why do you think the Union soldiers could use something like a

song to make them feel better?


» Given the challenging conditions, they needed ways to keep their spirits

up and to stay motivated to keep fighting.


3. **Literal.** What reasons motivated people to join the Union Army and fight in

the war?


» Because they thought it was their patriotic duty; because they believed

in the Union cause; because they thought the leaders of the Southern
states were committing treason and rebelling; because they were
opposed to slavery.


4. **Inferential.** Why did “Battle Hymn of the Republic” help motivate people in

the North to continue to make sacrifices needed to support the Union?


» The hymn reminded the people and soldiers of the Union that they

were fighting for a just or morally right cause, and people were willing to
make sacrifices if it meant doing the right thing.


Lesson 7 Battle Hymn of the Republic
**119**


~~**WORD WORK: EMANCIPATION (5 MIN.)**~~


1. In the Read-Aloud, you heard, “Others supported the idea of
emancipation, which means freeing a person from slavery.”


2. Say the word _emancipation_ with me.


3. _Emancipation_ means freeing a person from slavery.


4. Many enslaved people wanted emancipation.


5. The word _emancipation_ means freedom. What do you think _emancipation,_
_or freedom_, meant to enslaved people? _[Ask two or three students. If_
_necessary, guide responses.]_


6. What is the word we have been talking about? What part of speech is the
word _emancipation? (Noun)_


**Do a sharing activity for follow-up.** The word _emancipation_ means
freedom. Turn and talk to a partner about what freedom means to you.

#### Application

~~**TEKS 2.7.B; TEKS 2.7.C; TEKS 2.7.E**~~


~~**CIVIL WAR TIMELINE (5 MIN.)**~~


  - Tell students they will create a timeline to help them remember the sequence
of some important events connected to the Civil War.


  - Remind students that Abraham Lincoln was elected president in 1860. Place
Image Card 6 (Abraham Lincoln) on the Civil War timeline you prepared
earlier.


  - Ask students if they think Harriet Tubman gained her freedom before or after
Abraham Lincoln was elected president _(before Lincoln became president)._
Have students place Image Card 7 (Harriet Tubman) to the left of Image Card
6 to show that she gained her freedom before Abraham Lincoln became
president.


  - Show students Image Card 8 (the Confederacy). Ask students if these states
seceded and formed the Confederacy before or after Abraham Lincoln was
elected president _(after Lincoln was elected president)._ Ask a student to
place the image card in the correct location on the timeline to show that the
Confederacy formed after Abraham Lincoln was elected president.


  - Show students Image Card 9 (Julia Ward Howe). Ask students if Julia Ward
Howe wrote “Battle Hymn of the Republic” before or after the Confederacy
was formed and the Civil War began _(after the Civil War began)._


  - Save the Civil War timeline for future lessons.


~~**POETRY ANALYSIS (15 MIN.)**~~


  - Tell students they will take a look at the lyrics of “Battle Hymn of the
Republic” and pay close attention to the words used in the song. Then they
will complete a graphic organizer to analyze the poetic language in the song.


**TEKS 2.7.B** Write brief comments on literary or informational texts that demonstrate an understanding of the text; **TEKS**

illustrating or writing.

Support

that these song lyrics
helped Union soldiers

by reminding them of
the reason they were
fighting. The song
conveyed that the Union
soldiers were fighting
to carry out what God
would have them do,
which is to unite the
country and free the
slaves.

**Turn and Talk:** Discuss the following questions with your partner:


       - What did “Battle Hymn of the Republic” mean to the Union soldiers and

their families?


» Answers may vary but may include the following: “Battle Hymn of

the Republic” meant giving the Union soldiers hope and positivity

during very hard times. It served as a reminder of why they were

fighting the war.


       - What did Union soldiers sacrifice to fight in the Civil War?


» Many hundreds of thousands sacrificed their lives, and many

more were injured.

the Republic has been a popular song in America ever since the Civil War. To
close the lesson, show the video of the Muppets humming the Battle Hymn

**TEKS 2.1.A** Listen actively, ask relevant questions to clarify information, and answer questions using multi-word responses;
**TEKS 2.1.C** Share information and ideas that focus on the topic under discussion, speaking clearly at an appropriate pace
and using the conventions of language; **TEKS 2.6.G** Evaluate details read to determine key ideas; **TEKS 2.3.B** Use context
within and beyond a sentence to determine the meaning of unfamiliar words; **TEKS 2.7.B** Write brief comments on literary or
informational texts that demonstrate an understanding of the text; **TEKS 2.7.C** Use text evidence to support an appropriate
response; **TEKS 2.7.D** Retell and paraphrase texts in ways that maintain meaning and logical order; **TEKS 2.7.E** Interact with
sources in meaningful ways such as illustrating or writing.

» Abraham Lincoln

» Hampton, Virginia


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War

~~**CORE VOCABULARY**~~


**cabinet, n.** a group of people who give advice to the president; advisors

Example: President Lincoln’s _cabinet_ helped him make decisions.
Variation(s): cabinets


**proclamation, n.** an official announcement

Example: The president signed the _proclamation_ declaring the day a
national holiday.
Variation(s): proclamations


**scroll, n.** a rolled piece of parchment or paper

Example: Nadia rolled the note into a tiny _scroll_ and hid it behind her
bookshelf.
Variation(s): scrolls

**Start Lesson**


**Lesson 8: The Emancipation Proclamation**
#### Introducing the Read-Aloud



**Speaking and Listening:** Students will review information about what inspired

soldiers to continue fighting in the Civil War.


~~**TEKS 2.1.A; TEKS 2.1.C**~~


~~**WHAT HAVE WE ALREADY LEARNED? (5 MIN.)**~~


**Show Image Card 9: Julia Ward Howe**


Remind students that in the previous lesson, they analyzed the song “Battle
Hymn of the Republic.” Have students identify the woman in the image (Julia
Ward Howe) who wrote the song.


Prompt further discussion with the following questions:


  - How did Julia Ward Howe inspire soldiers to continue fighting in the Civil

War?


» Howe’s song “Battle Hymn of the Republic” inspired Union soldiers to

keep fighting in the Civil War by boosting their morale and reminding
them that they were fighting for a just and important cause.


  - What sacrifices did the Union soldiers make?


» Hundreds of thousands were injured or killed. There were also times

when they were tired, sick, hungry, cold, anxious, and homesick.


Check for Understanding


**Stand Up/Sit Down:** I am going to read you four statements. If the
statement that I read to you is true, stand up. If the statement that I read
is false, sit down, and share what would make the statement true.


**TEKS 2.1.A** Listen actively, ask relevant questions to clarify information, and answer questions using multi-word responses;
**TEKS 2.1.C** Share information and ideas that focus on the topic under discussion, speaking clearly at an appropriate pace
and using the conventions of language.



10m

**128**


       - Approximately 180,000 Black soldiers fought for the Union. (stand up)


       - Advancements in weaponry made the war very dangerous. (stand up)


       - “Battle Hymn of the Republic” became very popular among Confederate

troops. (sit down; It was popular among Union troops.)


       - Julia Ward Howe wrote “Battle Hymn of the Republic” before the Civil

War began. (sit down; She wrote it after the Civil War began.)


        - Julia Ward Howe believed in the cause of uniting the country. (stand up)


~~**ESSENTIAL BACKGROUND INFORMATION OR TERMS (5 MIN.)**~~


 - Explain that today’s Read-Aloud is about a very important speech, or
announcement, during the Civil War.


 - Explain that the word _proclamation_ is an official announcement.


 - Remind students they learned that Abraham Lincoln was elected president,
and he wanted to end slavery.


 - Read the title of the Read-Aloud to students.


 - Have students explain what the word _emancipation_ means. (the act of setting
free, or freeing a person from slavery)


 - Ask students what they think the announcement will be about based on the
name Emancipation Proclamation.

Lead students in a
brief discussion about
President Lincoln and
words that describe who
he was as a person.



Lesson 8 The Emancipation Proclamation

#### Read-Aloud


**Reading:** Students will explain one of the reasons why President Lincoln is

remembered as a courageous president.

8A-1–8A-8

**Language:** Students will demonstrate an understanding of the Tier 2 word _scroll._


~~**TEKS 2.3.B**~~


~~**PURPOSE FOR LISTENING**~~


  - Tell students to listen carefully to find out why President Lincoln is
remembered as a courageous president.


~~**“THE EMANCIPATION PROCLAMATION” (15 MIN.)**~~


**Show Image 8A-1: Samuel and Violet**
**Gathering with Others Around a Soldier**


~~_What do you think is happening in this picture?_~~


“Gather in closer! Closer everyone!” the soldier
cried out. The people gathered beneath a massive
~~_or very large_~~ oak tree. It was a bitter cold day in
January 1863. A 10-year-old boy named Samuel
and his little sister Violet squeezed and prodded their way through the crowd.


“Let’s keep going to the front,” Samuel said, tugging Violet’s hand.


“No, let’s stay here in the middle where it’s warm,” said Violet, thankful to have so
many bodies pressed close around to shield her from the freezing January wind.


**Show Image 8A-2: Soldier Holding Up a Scroll**


“Fine, you stay here. But I’m going up front so I
can hear,” said Samuel.


“You don’t even know what he’s going to talk
about,” Violet said as her brother wormed his
way toward the tree.


**TEKS 2.6.G** Evaluate details read to determine key ideas; **TEKS 2.3.B** Use context within and beyond a sentence to determine
the meaning of unfamiliar words.



Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**130**


Finally, Violet gave in and followed her brother to the very front. There, a
Union soldier, wearing a long, heavy blue coat, held a scroll, a rolled piece of
paper.


“What is it, some kind of news?” asked a woman in the crowd.


“What happened? What’s on that piece of paper?” asked another. Everyone
wanted to hear the announcement. ~~_What do you think the scroll will say?_~~


**Show Image 8A-3 Soldier Reading from the**
**Scroll**


The tree where the people were gathering was
in a place called Hampton, Virginia. ~~[Display~~
~~the U.S. Map and point to Virginia.]~~ ~~_Was_~~
~~_Virginia a Union or Confederate state?_~~


Hampton was different from other places in
Virginia. Fort Monroe, which was in Hampton, was still under Union control,
unlike Fort Sumter and so many other forts controlled by the Confederates.
The Union soldiers also controlled the nearby port and town. During the
war, many escaped slaves came to Fort Monroe. They hoped to be safe from
catchers. Eventually, a community of free African Americans sprung up
around the town of Hampton. That is why, on this day in 1863, a large group
of free African Americans were gathering under the giant oak tree in Virginia
to listen to the Union soldier. ~~_So, how was this part of Virginia different from_~~
~~_other parts of Virginia and other Confederate states?_~~


Samuel and Violet had been born into slavery. They and their parents
escaped at the beginning of the war, and they had been living in Hampton
ever since.


“Quiet!” the soldier called. “Quiet, please!”


When everyone was settled, the soldier read the first words: “By the
President of the United States of America: A **Proclamation** .” ~~_This means it is_~~
~~_an official announcement from President Lincoln._~~


The soldier waited a moment. Everyone knew right away that they were
about to hear something very important. It was not every day that the
president sent out written proclamations. The soldier continued reading.


“Whereas, on the twenty-second day of September, in the year of our Lord
one thousand eight hundred and sixty-two, a proclamation was issued by the
President of the United States, containing, among other things, the following,
to wit . . .”


Lesson 8 The Emancipation Proclamation
**131**


**Show Image 8A-4 People Cheering: Samuel**
**and Violet Trying to Understand**


“What did he just say?” Violet asked eagerly,
tugging on Samuel’s sleeve.


“I’m not really sure,” he replied. “It was just a
fancy way of saying the date, I think. ~~_The date_~~
~~_was September 22, 1862._~~ Now let’s listen!”


The soldier read some more: “That on the first day of January . . . all persons
held as slaves within any State or designated part of a State . . . shall be then,
thenceforward, and forever free.”


The crowd gasped with relief and joy. “Read that again!” someone shouted,
interrupting the soldier. “I want to make sure I heard you right.” Many people
in the crowd had been enslaved at one time. They were very happy to hear
that President Lincoln was proclaiming an end to slavery.


Samuel and Violet listened to the rest of it, but when it was over, Violet did
not really understand most of what she had heard. “So, does this mean that
we don’t have to worry about being captured by the catchers anymore?” she
asked Samuel.


“I think so,” Samuel answered, rubbing his chin. “I think President Lincoln
said all the slaves are now free, but I’m not really sure. We’ll have to ask
Mother what it all means.”


**Show Image 8A-5: President Lincoln and the**
**Emancipation Proclamation**


The document the soldier read is one of the
most famous documents in the history of the
United States. It is called the Emancipation
Proclamation. ~~Emancipation~~ ~~_is the act of_~~
~~_setting something or someone free. What_~~
~~_is a proclamation?_~~ The Emancipation
Proclamation freed slaves in the South. But in the United States, a President
can’t act alone. Congress needed to enact laws to abolish, or end, slavery.
A few years after Lincoln issued the Emancipation Proclamation, Congress
did something important: It changed the Constitution to abolish slavery
permanently. Slavery was eventually abolished ~~_or ended_~~ in all states;
however, this didn’t happen only because of the Emancipation Proclamation.


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**132**


**Show Image 8A-6: Lincoln Reading the**
**Emancipation Proclamation Before His**
**Cabinet**


Slavery was a major reason that the nation
was divided in the first place. But the reason
President Lincoln declared war was that
the Confederacy broke national law by
seceding from the Union. In other words, in
the beginning, the Civil War was focused on keeping the nation united. The
Emancipation Proclamation changed this.


The Emancipation Proclamation changed the focus and purpose of the war
at a time when things were not going well for the Union. This was before
the Battle of Gettysburg, which took place in July 1863. The Union had
not won many battles, yet tens of thousands of men were already dead or
wounded. There was no end in sight to the war. ~~_So what did the Emancipation_~~
~~_Proclamation do and not do? (It changed the war, but it didn’t end the war.)_~~


Many people, including his closest advisors, wanted President Lincoln to end
the war and let the Confederacy have its way. This picture shows Lincoln
just having read the Proclamation to his cabinet or group of advisors. Some
thought it was a good idea, and some did not, but President Lincoln did
what he knew was the right thing to do. The Emancipation Proclamation let
everyone know that not only was President Lincoln determined to preserve,
or save, the Union, but also he wanted to make sure that slavery would never
cause another war. This made the abolitionists very happy, and after that,
they put their full support behind President Lincoln and the war. ~~_Who were_~~
~~_abolitionists?_~~


**Show Image 8A-7: The Spirit of Freedom**
**Sculpture**


As another result of the Emancipation
Proclamation, free African Americans and
escaped slaves began to fight for the Union.
The famous abolitionist Frederick Douglass
helped recruit African Americans. This
means that he encouraged them to join the
Union Army as soldiers. Many African Americans did join the Union Army
and fought courageously. African American men and women were eager ~~_or_~~
~~_excited_~~ to help the Union Army in other ways, too. ~~_Why do you think African_~~
~~_Americans wanted to fight in the Civil War or help support the Union?_~~ Men
worked as carpenters, cooks, guards, laborers, and boat pilots. African
American women were nurses, spies, and scouts. Men and women who



Support


Reread this sentence
from the Read-Aloud:
“Some thought it was
a good idea, and some
did not, but President
Lincoln did what he
knew was the right
thing to do.” Explain that
President Lincoln stood
up for what he knew
was right—abolishing
slavery and making sure
it would never cause
another war—even when
his closest advisors and
others did not agree with
him.



Lesson 8 The Emancipation Proclamation
**133**


had never been enslaved, along with formerly enslaved African Americans,
bravely worked together. They did this to save the Union and ensure that
enslaved people were free. ~~_This statue, the Spirit of Freedom, stands today_~~
~~_at the African American Civil War Memorial Museum in Washington, D.C. It_~~
~~_honors the more than 180,000 African-American soldiers who served the_~~
~~_United States during the Civil War._~~


**Show Image 8A-8: Emancipation Oak**


From that day forward, the great oak tree
in Hampton, Virginia, became known as
Emancipation Oak. It was the first place
the Emancipation Proclamation was read in
Confederate territory. After the Emancipation
Proclamation, the Union Army freed slaves
each time it won a battle and took control of
a town or some farmland in a Southern state. Little by little, slavery in the
United States was finally coming to an end.


Check for Understanding


**With a Partner:** With your partner, write at least one reason President
Lincoln is considered a courageous president. [Have several students
share their responses with the class.]


» Answers may vary but may include that issuing the Emancipation

Proclamation was courageous because, at the time, some of

his advisors were suggesting he just end the war and let the

Confederacy have its way. President Lincoln instead chose to

issue the Emancipation Proclamation and try to keep the Union

together.


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**134**


~~**COMPREHENSION QUESTIONS (10 MIN.)**~~


1. **Literal.** What important event did Samuel and Violet observe?


» the reading of the Emancipation Proclamation


2. **Literal.** What did the Emancipation Proclamation do?


» It changed the focus of the war and eventually led to enslaved people

being freed; it allowed African American soldiers to fight in the Union
Army.


3. **Literal.** Who wrote the Emancipation Proclamation?


» President Lincoln


4. **Inferential.** Why do you think a Union soldier read the Emancipation
Proclamation rather than a Confederate soldier?


» The Union supported the position of the Emancipation Proclamation
and freeing the enslaved people.


5. **Evaluative.** _Think-Pair-Share:_ Abraham Lincoln wrote the Emancipation
Proclamation while the Union wasn’t winning the war and many disagreed
with his beliefs about slavery. What might this say about who he was?


» Answers may vary but may include the following: He had strong morals;
he was a good person or someone who does the right thing. He was
brave to stand up for what he believed was right, even at a time when
the odds were against the Union.


6. **Evaluative.** [Show Image 8A-8: Emancipation Oak] Do you think
Emancipation Oak is a good name for this tree? Why or why not?


» Answers may vary but may include the following: Yes, because this
commemorates where the Emancipation Proclamation was read.


7. **Evaluative.** _Think-Pair-Share:_ What do you think Harriet Tubman might have
said when she heard about the Emancipation Proclamation?


» Answers may vary but may include the following: Harriet Tubman would
be happy because, as an abolitionist, she worked very hard to help free
the slaves.

~~**MULTIPLE-MEANING WORD ACTIVITY: SCROLL (5 MIN.)**~~


**Show Poster 1M (Scroll)**


 - Tell students they will hold up one or two fingers to indicate which image

shows the meaning of the word being discussed.


 - Remind students that they heard in the Read-Aloud, “There, a Union soldier,
wearing a long, heavy blue coat, held a _scroll_, a rolled piece of paper.” (one
finger)


 - Explain that _scroll_ can also mean other things, like to move a graphic on
a screen, like scrolling on your tablet. Ask which picture matches this
description of _scroll_ . (two fingers)


 - Have students work with a partner to quiz each other on the different
meanings of the word _scroll._ Remind them to be as descriptive as possible
and use complete sentences.


**Use a Stand Up/Sit Down activity for follow-up.**


I am going to read four sentences. If I describe a rolled piece of paper, stand
up. If I describe moving a graphic on a screen, sit down.


 - Katie scrolls on her phone to find her favorite picture. (sit down)


 - Let’s go on a treasure hunt and see if we can find an old pirate’s scroll with a
map on it! (stand up)


 - The Union soldier read the announcement on the scroll. (stand up)


 - I love to scroll through funny cat videos on my mom’s phone! (sit down)

Lesson 9 Robert E. Lee
**141**


~~**ADVANCE PREPARATION**~~


**Introducing the Read-Aloud**


                 - Prepare to display Flip Book Image 8A-5 during the review.


**Read-Aloud**


                 - Prepare to display and refer to the U.S Map during the reading or prepare to
use an alternate U.S. map for support.


**Application**


                - Prepare to display and use Somebody, Wanted, But, So, Then to model, or
recreate a similar chart to use.


**Universal Access**


**Introducing the Read-Aloud**


                 - Assign small groups to discuss their learning about the Emancipation
Proclamation before beginning the Check for Understanding activity.


**Read-Aloud**


                 - Provide a graphic organizer for students to record details about the Union
and Confederate armies during the Read-Aloud.


**Application**


                - Prepare Somebody, Wanted, But, So, Then charts on chart paper to provide
opportunities for students to move around the classroom. When partners
have recorded their thinking on the chart paper, invite students to participate
in a brief Gallery Walk to review the class’s thinking.


~~**CORE VOCABULARY**~~


**advisors, n.** people who give suggestions or advice

Example: I asked my parents to be _advisors_ when I couldn’t decide whether
or not to play on the soccer team.
Variation(s): advisor


**frail, adj.** weak and fragile

Example: Daniel helps his _frail_ grandfather take care of his garden.
Variation(s): none


**general, n.** a military officer of high rank or position

Example: The army _general_ had many awards decorating her uniform.
Variation(s): generals


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**142**


**oath, n.** a promise made before witnesses

Example: Before you testify in court, you must take an _oath_ to tell the truth.
Variation(s): oaths


**wasteland, n.** an area that is devastated, or destroyed, by something, such
as a flood, storm, or war

Example: The first battle of the Civil War turned the city of Manassas,
Virginia, into a _wasteland._
Variation(s): wastelands

**Recall:** Use the following questions to review what students have learned
about the Emancipation Proclamation:


        - Who issued the Emancipation Proclamation?


» President Lincoln


        - How did the Emancipation Proclamation change the focus of the war?


» It placed more of the focus on ending slavery, rather than just

reuniting the country.


        - Did the Emancipation Proclamation end the Civil War?


» No.


**TEKS 2.1.D** Work collaboratively with others by following agreed-upon rules for discussion, including listening to others,
speaking when recognized, making appropriate contributions, and building on the ideas of others.

- Did the Emancipation Proclamation abolish slavery permanently in the

United States?


» No, Congress changed the Constitution to permanently end

slavery in the United States a few years later.


- How did the Emancipation Proclamation help the Union Army?


» As a result of the proclamation, free African Americans and

escaped slaves began to fight for the Union Army.

~~_What do you see in this picture?_~~


After two days of fighting, the cornfields
around Gettysburg, Pennsylvania, were all
destroyed, so that one could hardly tell that
there had been crops there at all. ~~[Display the~~
~~U.S Map and point to Gettysburg.]~~ Green,
grassy pastures trampled by so many boots and horse hooves were now
nothing but mud. The trees in the forests had lost their leaves, and many
were burned or simply blown to bits by cannonballs. In short, all around
Gettysburg was a **wasteland,** ~~_an area that is devastated or destroyed,_~~ but the
battle was not over yet. ~~_How does the image of a_~~ ~~wasteland~~ ~~_help you_~~
~~_understand what might have happened at Gettysburg?_~~

It was past midnight. The date was July 3,
1863.


Two Confederate soldiers stood guard outside
the door of a small stone farmhouse at the

“Should we see if he is ready to issue orders?”


“No, don’t bother him. The Old Man will let us know when he is ready,” said
another.


Inside the house, a man stood hunched over a table, studying a map by
candlelight. He was not a very old man—just 56 years old—but constant
war and worries had brought new wrinkles to his face. He was **frail,** and far
thinner than he had been just two years before. But all the soldiers loved
**General** Lee as though he were their own father. They called him “The Old
Man” out of respect. ~~_A_~~ ~~general~~ ~~_is a military officer in charge of soldiers._~~
~~_General Lee was in charge of the Confederate Army._~~


**Show Image 9A-3: Lee Family Home**


General Lee’s full name was Robert E. Lee.
General Lee was born in 1807. He was the son
of a hero from the Revolutionary War who had
fought bravely alongside George Washington
to make America free from Great Britain.


Robert E. Lee joined the army at age 17 and
graduated second in his class from the United States Military Academy. ~~_He_~~
~~_had the second-highest scores in his class, which means he did very well in_~~
~~_school._~~ Then Lee served in the U.S. Army during the Mexican-American War.


Lee was proud to serve in the U.S. Army before the Civil War, but he was
born and raised in Virginia, a Confederate state. ~~[Point to Virginia on the U.S.~~
~~Map.]~~ Lee married Mary Custis, a great-granddaughter of George and Martha
Washington. After they married, Robert and Mary lived in Mary’s plantation
home known as Arlington House.


Lee did not think the South should secede from the Union. ~~_Can you recall_~~
~~_what secede means?_~~ Like many other people, he wanted to find a peaceful
way to end the disagreement, and he swore he would never break the **oath** ~~_or_~~
~~_promise_~~ he had taken to uphold the U.S. Constitution. ~~_As a soldier, he made_~~
~~_an_~~ ~~oath~~ ~~_, or promise, to do what was best for the United States._~~
