Image Cards



Unit Review
**203**


Have students do a Question-Pair-Share for each image card. For example,
for the picture of Harriet Tubman, a student might ask, “What is Harriet
Tubman famous for doing?”


**You Were There: Reading of the Emancipation Proclamation,**
**Surrender at Appomattox**

                 - Have students pretend that they were at one of the important events during
the Civil War. Ask students to describe what they saw and heard. For example,
for the reading of the Emancipation Proclamation, students may talk about
seeing the soldier, the scroll, etc. They may talk about hearing people
cheering, clapping, etc. Consider also extending this activity by adding group
or independent writing opportunities associated with the “You Were There”
concept. For example, ask students to pretend they are newspaper reporters
describing the surrender at Appomattox Court House and write a group news
article describing the event.


**Class Book: The U.S. Civil War**

Materials: Drawing paper, drawing tools


                 - Tell the class or a group of students that they are going to make a class book
to help them remember what they have learned in this unit. Have students
brainstorm important information about the Civil War, Robert E. Lee, Clara
Barton, the Emancipation Proclamation, Ulysses S. Grant, and Abraham
Lincoln. Have each student choose one idea to draw a picture of and then
write a caption for the picture. Bind the pages to make a book to put in the
class library for students to review.


**Using a Map**

                 - Use the Global Map to review different global locations mentioned in the
Read-Alouds. Ask questions such as the following:


                        - Where was slavery practiced around the world? _(Ancient Greece, Mali,_
_Central and South America)_


                        - Where did Clara Barton work as a nurse outside of the United States, before
returning to start the American Red Cross? _(Europe, Turkey, China, Cuba)_


                 - Use the U.S. Map to review various U.S. locations from the Read-Alouds. Ask
questions such as the following:


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**204**


    - The bombardment of Fort Sumter near Charleston, South Carolina,

marked the beginning of the Civil War. Can anyone find South Carolina on
the map? Was South Carolina part of the North or the South? _(the South)_


    - General Lee surrendered to General Grant at the village of Appomattox

Court House, Virginia. Can anyone find Virginia on the map? Was Virginia
part of the North or the South? _(South)_


    - One of the major battles of the Civil War was at Gettysburg, Pennsylvania.

Can anyone find Pennsylvania on the map? Was Pennsylvania part of the
North or the South? _(the North)_


**Compare/Contrast**

  - Tell students that there are many things to compare and contrast in the
Read-Alouds they have heard so far. Remind students that to compare means
to tell how things or people are similar and to contrast is to tell how things or
people are different. Have students choose a topic from the following list to
compare/contrast on a chart. You may do this individually or as a class.


    - General Ulysses S. Grant and General Robert E. Lee


    - Harriet Tubman and Clara Barton


    - The Northern economy and the Southern economy


    - Yankees and Rebels


  - You may wish to extend this activity by using the chart as a prewriting tool
and having students write two paragraphs, one describing similarities and
the other describing differences.


**T-Chart**

  - Have students create a T-Chart, with the left column labeled “Costs” and the
right column labeled “Outcomes.” Invite them to use the T-Chart to record as
many costs and outcomes of the Civil War as they can from the Read-Alouds.


  - You may wish to extend this activity by inviting students to select one cost or
outcome to explain to the class, including who was most affected by it.


**Sequencing Events**

  - Have students use Activity Page UR.1 to sequence events related to the
Civil War. Activity Page UR.1


Unit Review
**205**


Activity Page UA.1



**Grade 2 | Unit 7**
### Unit Assessment


This Unit Assessment evaluates each student’s retention of unit and
academic vocabulary words and the core content targeted in _The U.S. Civil_
_War._ The results should guide review and remediation the following day.


There are three parts to this assessment. You may choose to do the
parts in more than one sitting if you feel this is more appropriate for your
students. Part I (Vocabulary Assessment) is divided into two sections: the
first assesses unit-related vocabulary, and the second assesses academic
vocabulary. Parts II and III of the assessment address the core content
targeted in _The U.S. Civil War._


~~**PART I**~~ ~~**TEKS 2.6.F**~~


**Directions:** I am going to ask a question about a word you have heard in the
Read-Alouds. First I will say the word and then I will ask the question. If the
answer to the question is _yes_, circle the thumbs-up symbol. If the answer to
the question is _no_, circle the thumbs-down symbol. I will ask each question
twice.


1. **Plantations:** Are plantations large farms where crops are grown and
harvested by enslaved laborers? _(thumbs-up)_


2. **Slavery:** In slavery, is an enslaved person paid for their work? _(thumbs-_
_down)_


3. **Great Awakenings:** Were the Great Awakenings religious movements that
influenced leaders who fought to end slavery? _(thumbs-up)_


4. **Economy:** Did the Northern economy depend more on factories and the
Southern economy depend more on growing crops? _(thumbs-up)_


5. **Confederacy:** Was the Confederacy the collection of Northern states that
did not secede from the United States? _(thumbs-down)_


6. **Union:** Was the Union made up of Northern states that did not secede from
the United States? _(thumbs-up)_


7. **Civil war:** Is a civil war a war between regions within one country? _(thumbs-_
_up)_


8. **Rebels:** Were the Rebels the Union soldiers? _(thumbs-down)_


**TEKS 2.6.F** Make inferences and use evidence to support understanding.


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War



**206**


9. **Emancipation Proclamation:** Did the Emancipation Proclamation say that
slavery would be abolished in the Confederate states? _(thumbs-up)_


10. **Yankees:** Did the Yankees fight for the Union? _(thumbs-up)_


11. **Value:** Does a pencil have great value when you are trying to take a test?
_(thumbs-up)_


12. **Compromise:** When two people argue, do they reach a compromise if they
work together to find a middle ground? _(thumbs-up)_


13. **Contributions:** Was one of Clara Barton’s major contributions to the Union

effort that she became a conductor on the Underground Railroad? _(thumbs-_
_down)_


14. **Advisors:** Did President Lincoln use many advisors to help him make
decisions during his presidency? _(thumbs-up)_


15. **Defeat:** When a team loses a game, can we say they experienced a defeat?
_(thumbs-up)_


~~**PART II**~~


~~**TEKS 2.6.F**~~
**Directions:** Let’s read the names in each row together. I will read a sentence
about one of the people you learned about related to the U.S. Civil War. You
will circle the name of the person I am describing.


1. I was president during the U.S. Civil War. (Barton, _Lincoln_, Grant)


2. I commanded the Confederate Army. ( _Lee_, Grant, Tubman)


3. I was a conductor on the Underground Railroad. (Barton, _Tubman_,
Lincoln)


4. I commanded the Union Army. (Barton, _Grant_, Lee)


5. I helped wounded soldiers get the medical supplies and care they
needed. (Lincoln, Tubman, _Barton_ )


6. I wrote the Emancipation Proclamation. ( _Lincoln_, Grant, Lee)


7. I was an enslaved African American who escaped to Pennsylvania to
gain my freedom. (Barton, _Tubman_, Grant)


8. I surrendered to General Grant at Appomattox. (Lincoln, Tubman, _Lee_ )


9. I founded the American Red Cross. ( _Barton_, Tubman, Lincoln)


10. I was killed by John Wilkes Booth after the end of the Civil War. ( _Lincoln_,
Grant, Lee)


**TEKS 2.6.F** Make inferences and use evidence to support understanding.

~~**PART III**~~ ~~**TEKS 2.6.G; TEKS 2.7.B**~~


**Directions:** Write a complete sentence to answer each question or
statement.


1. How did the abolitionists feel about slavery?


2. What was the Underground Railroad?


3. What caused the U.S. Civil War?


4. List two ways that the North and the South were different.


5. Why did some Southern states secede, or break away, from the

United States?


6. What did the end of the U.S. Civil War mean for the North and the South and

for slaves?


7. What was the most interesting thing you learned about the

U.S. Civil War?


**TEKS 2.6.G** Evaluate details read to determine key ideas; **TEKS 2.7.B** Write brief comments on literary or informational
texts that demonstrate an understanding of the text.

**Grade 2 | Unit 7**
#### Culminating Activities


~~**NOTE TO TEACHER**~~


Please use this final day to address the class results of the Unit Assessment.
Based on the results of the Unit Assessment, you may wish to use this class
time to provide remediation opportunities that target specific areas of
weakness for individual students, small groups, or the whole class.


Alternatively, you may also choose to use this class time to extend or enrich
students’ experience with unit knowledge. A number of enrichment activities
are provided below in order to provide students with opportunities to enliven
their experiences with unit concepts.


~~**REMEDIATION**~~


You may choose to regroup students according to a particular area of
weakness, as indicated from Unit Assessment results.


Remediation opportunities include the following:


                  - Targeting Review activities


                  - Revisiting lesson applications


                - Rereading and discussing select Read-Alouds


~~**ENRICHMENT**~~


**Key Vocabulary Brainstorming**


Materials: Board or chart paper


                 - Give students a key unit concept or vocabulary word such as _Yankees_,
_abolitionists_, or _rebels_ . Have them brainstorm everything that comes to mind
when they hear the word, such as _North_, _Union_, etc. Record their responses
on the board or chart paper for reference.


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**210**


**Class Book: The U.S. Civil War**


Materials: Drawing paper, drawing tools


  - Tell the class or a group of students that they are going to make a class book
to help them remember what they have learned in this unit. Have students
brainstorm important information about the Civil War, Harriet Tubman,
Robert E. Lee, Clara Barton, the Emancipation Proclamation, Abraham
Lincoln, and Ulysses S. Grant. Have each student choose one idea to draw a
picture of and then write a caption for the picture. Bind the pages to make a
book to put in the class library for students to read again and again.


**Civil War Journals**
Materials: Students’ Civil War Journals, binding materials (optional)


  - Students may share and discuss their Civil War journal entries with a partner
or with the class. You may wish to bind students’ individual journals now that
they are complete.


**Student Choice**


Materials: Unit Read-Aloud texts


  - Ask students which Read-Aloud they have heard recently that they would like
to hear again. If necessary, reread the titles of recent Read-Alouds to refresh
students’ memories. You may also want to choose one yourself.


  - Reread the selected text. Feel free to pause at different places in the ReadAloud this time and talk about vocabulary and information you did not
discuss previously during the Read-Aloud.


  - After the Read-Aloud, ask students if they noticed anything new or different
during the second reading that they did not notice during the first reading.
Also, ask them to try to express why they like this Read-Aloud. Remember
to repeat and expand upon each response using richer and more complex
language, including, if possible, any Read-Aloud vocabulary.


**Writing Prompts**


Materials: Writing paper, pencil


  - Students may be given an additional writing prompt such as the following:


    - If I could interview Harriet Tubman, I would ask her . . .


    - Clara Barton was a brave woman because . . .


    - Abraham Lincoln was a great president because . . .


    - When the Civil War ended . . .


    - If I could meet any person from Civil War times, I would want to meet . . .


Culminating Activities
**211**


**Letter to a Friend**


Materials: Writing paper, pencil


                 - Have students choose a historical figure from the texts they’ve studied and
write a letter to a friend from that person’s perspective. Encourage students
to write the letter to someone with whom they share a connection. Remind
students that these people were friendly with each other, so their letters
should reflect that warmth and familiarity.


Historical Connections:


                        - Abraham Lincoln and Ulysses S. Grant


                        - Harriet Tubman and Frederick Douglass


                        - Clara Barton and a Union soldier


**Perspective**


Materials: Writing paper, pencil


                - Have students choose a character from the Read-Alouds they have heard
so far. Tell them that they are going to write two or three sentences about
the war from that character’s perspective. Remind them that perspective is
how someone sees or experiences something. Elaborate, saying that Clara
Barton’s perspective of the Civil War would have been very different from
Confederate President Jefferson Davis’s perspective. Prompt them with
questions such as “What does your character think about the war? Is your
character involved in the war? How?”


                        - Clara Barton


                        - General Grant


                        - General Lee


                        - A civilian watching the Battle of Manassas


                        - Confederate President Jefferson Davis


                        - A former enslaved person who escaped to the North


                 - Allow students to share their writing with the class and ask each other
questions. Remember to expand on each student’s response using richer and
more complex language, including, if possible, any Read-Aloud vocabulary.


**Red Cross Volunteer**

                 - Invite a volunteer from the local Red Cross to come in and talk with your
class about the work that they do with the Red Cross. You may help your
students formulate questions to ask the guest speaker.


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War
**212**


**Research Activity**

  - If any questions were left unanswered about the Northern or Southern
economies, Southern plantations, the Underground Railroad, or the evolution
of flags during the Civil War, give students the opportunity to continue their
research on these topics. Encourage students to present their findings to a
group of students or the class.


**Research Activity: Evolution of Flags During the Civil War**

  - Review the Union and the Confederacy and what caused the states that
formed the Confederacy to secede, thus causing the Civil War.


  - Review the images of the Union and Confederate flags from Lesson 6. Review
the states that are a part of the Union.


  - Likewise, review the states that seceded from the Union at different points
and how that corresponds to the number of stars on the two Confederate
flags.


  - Have students research images of the Union and Confederate flags during

the time of the Civil War and how they evolved throughout the Civil War,
including different battle flags.


  - Encourage students to share their findings in groups or as a class

presentation.


**Make a Proclamation Scroll**


Materials: Empty paper towel tubes, kraft paper or construction paper, white
glue, pencils or markers


  - Remind students that a proclamation is an official announcement, and
Abraham Lincoln wrote the Emancipation Proclamation to help end slavery.


  - Encourage students to think about a proclamation (announcement) they
would like to make to their classmates.


  - Distribute construction paper and encourage students to write their
proclamation on the paper.


  - Consider providing the following prompts for announcements:


    - Attention, everyone! I have some exciting news to share!


    - Dear classmates, I have something important to tell you . . .


    - I’m here to announce . . .


    - Hello, friends! I’m excited to share this news with you today . . .


  - Once they’ve written their proclamations, assist students with gluing one


Culminating Activities
**213**


paper towel roll to the top edge of the paper and one paper towel roll to the
bottom edge.


                   - Assist students as they roll the paper around the top and bottom paper towel
rolls until they meet in the middle.


                   - After students have finished assembling their scrolls, encourage students to
share their proclamations with the class.


**Gallery Walk**


Materials: Image Cards, tape


                  - Tape each image card from the unit to the walls throughout the classroom.
Encourage students to walk around the classroom to observe the images.
After all students have circulated and observed the image cards, assign them
to small groups to discuss what they observed. Provide students with the
following prompts to focus their discussion:


                          - I observed . . .


                          - I recalled . . . about this image.


                          - The things I found interesting about this image were . . .


                          - I have a question about . . .


                  - Bring students together and encourage them to share something they talked
about in their groups with the whole class.


Unit 7 The U.S. Civil War

|“The Great Awakenings and<br>the Founders”|• 1,257 words<br>• Vocabulary includes academic language such as compromise<br>• The text introduces simple factual information alongside the narrative<br>• Illustrations support the text but requires some interpretation<br>• Slightly longer passages with plain fonts and moderate spacing|

|<br> <br>(4) Cross-curricular second language acquisition/reading. The ELL reads a variety of texts for a variety of purposes with an<br>increasing level of comprehension in all content areas. ELLs may be at the beginning, intermediate, advanced, or advanced<br>high stage of English language acquisition in reading. In order for the ELL to meet grade-level learning expectations across the<br>foundation and enrichment curriculum, all instruction delivered in English must be linguistically accommodated (communicated,<br>sequenced, and scaffolded) commensurate with the student’s level of English language proficiency. For kindergarten and grade<br>1, certain of these student expectations apply to text read aloud for students not yet at the stage of decoding written text. The<br>student is expected to:|<br> <br>(4) Cross-curricular second language acquisition/reading. The ELL reads a variety of texts for a variety of purposes with an<br>increasing level of comprehension in all content areas. ELLs may be at the beginning, intermediate, advanced, or advanced<br>high stage of English language acquisition in reading. In order for the ELL to meet grade-level learning expectations across the<br>foundation and enrichment curriculum, all instruction delivered in English must be linguistically accommodated (communicated,<br>sequenced, and scaffolded) commensurate with the student’s level of English language proficiency. For kindergarten and grade<br>1, certain of these student expectations apply to text read aloud for students not yet at the stage of decoding written text. The<br>student is expected to:|<br> <br>(4) Cross-curricular second language acquisition/reading. The ELL reads a variety of texts for a variety of purposes with an<br>increasing level of comprehension in all content areas. ELLs may be at the beginning, intermediate, advanced, or advanced<br>high stage of English language acquisition in reading. In order for the ELL to meet grade-level learning expectations across the<br>foundation and enrichment curriculum, all instruction delivered in English must be linguistically accommodated (communicated,<br>sequenced, and scaffolded) commensurate with the student’s level of English language proficiency. For kindergarten and grade<br>1, certain of these student expectations apply to text read aloud for students not yet at the stage of decoding written text. The<br>student is expected to:|
|ELPS 4.A|<br> <br> <br>learn relationships between sounds and letters of the<br>English language and decode (sound out) words using<br>a combination of skills such as recognizing sound-<br>letter relationships and identifying cognates, affixes,<br>roots, and base words||
|ELPS 4.B|<br>recognize directionality of English reading such as left<br>to right and top to bottom||
|ELPS 4.C|<br> <br> <br>develop basic sight vocabulary, derive meaning<br>of environmental print, and comprehend English<br>vocabulary and language structures used routinely in<br>written classroom materials||
|ELPS 4.D|<br> <br> <br>use prereading supports such as graphic organizers,<br>illustrations, and pretaught topic-related vocabulary<br>and other prereading activities to enhance<br>comprehension of written text||
|ELPS 4.E|<br> <br>read linguistically accommodated content area<br>material with a decreasing need for linguistic<br>accommodations as more English is learned||
|ELPS 4.F|<br> <br> <br> <br> <br>use visual and contextual support and support from<br>peers and teachers to read grade-appropriate content<br>area text, enhance and confirm understanding, and<br>develop vocabulary, grasp of language structures,<br>and background knowledge needed to comprehend<br>increasingly challenging language||
|ELPS 4.G|<br> <br> <br> <br>demonstrate comprehension of increasingly complex<br>English by participating in shared reading, retelling or<br>summarizing material, responding to questions, and<br>taking notes commensurate with content area and<br>grade level needs||
|ELPS 4.H|<br>read silently with increasing ease and comprehension<br>for longer periods||
|ELPS 4.I|<br> <br> <br> <br> <br> <br>demonstrate English comprehension and expand<br>reading skills by employing basic reading skills such<br>as demonstrating understanding of supporting ideas<br>and details in text and graphic sources, summarizing<br>text, and distinguishing main ideas from details<br>commensurate with content area needs||

|Unit 5|Col2|Correlation—Teacher’s Guide|
|---|---|---|
|ELPS 4.J|<br> <br> <br> <br> <br>demonstrate English comprehension and expand<br>reading skills by employing inferential skills such<br>as predicting, making connections between ideas,<br>drawing inferences and conclusions from text and<br>graphic sources, and finding supporting text evidence<br>commensurate with content area needs||
|ELPS 4.K|<br>  <br> <br>  <br>demonstrate English comprehension and expand<br>reading skills by employing analytical skills such<br>as evaluating written information and performing<br>critical analyses commensurate with content area<br>and grade-level needs||
|<br> <br>(5) Cross-curricular second language acquisition/writing. The ELL writes in a variety of forms with increasing accuracy to<br>effectively address a specific purpose and audience in all content areas. ELLs may be at the beginning, intermediate, advanced,<br>or advanced high stage of English language acquisition in writing. In order for the ELL to meet grade-level learning expectations<br>across foundation and enrichment curriculum, all instruction delivered in English must be linguistically accommodated<br>(communicated, sequenced, and scaffolded) commensurate with the student’s level of English language proficiency. For<br>kindergarten and grade 1, certain of these student expectations do not apply until the student has reached the stage of generating<br>original written text using a standard writing system. The student is expected to:|<br> <br>(5) Cross-curricular second language acquisition/writing. The ELL writes in a variety of forms with increasing accuracy to<br>effectively address a specific purpose and audience in all content areas. ELLs may be at the beginning, intermediate, advanced,<br>or advanced high stage of English language acquisition in writing. In order for the ELL to meet grade-level learning expectations<br>across foundation and enrichment curriculum, all instruction delivered in English must be linguistically accommodated<br>(communicated, sequenced, and scaffolded) commensurate with the student’s level of English language proficiency. For<br>kindergarten and grade 1, certain of these student expectations do not apply until the student has reached the stage of generating<br>original written text using a standard writing system. The student is expected to:|<br> <br>(5) Cross-curricular second language acquisition/writing. The ELL writes in a variety of forms with increasing accuracy to<br>effectively address a specific purpose and audience in all content areas. ELLs may be at the beginning, intermediate, advanced,<br>or advanced high stage of English language acquisition in writing. In order for the ELL to meet grade-level learning expectations<br>across foundation and enrichment curriculum, all instruction delivered in English must be linguistically accommodated<br>(communicated, sequenced, and scaffolded) commensurate with the student’s level of English language proficiency. For<br>kindergarten and grade 1, certain of these student expectations do not apply until the student has reached the stage of generating<br>original written text using a standard writing system. The student is expected to:|
|ELPS 5.A|<br>  <br>learn relationships between sounds and letters of the<br>English language to represent sounds when writing<br>in English||
|ELPS 5.B|<br>write using newly acquired basic vocabulary and<br>content-based grade-level vocabulary||
|ELPS 5.C|<br> <br>spell familiar English words with increasing accuracy,<br>and employ English spelling patterns and rules with<br>increasing accuracy as more English is acquired||
|ELPS 5.D|<br> <br> <br>edit writing for standard grammar and usage,<br>including subject-verb agreement, pronoun agreement,<br>and appropriate verb tenses commensurate with<br>grade-level expectations as more English is acquired||
|ELPS 5.E|<br> <br> <br> <br>employ increasingly complex grammatical structures<br>in content area writing commensurate with grade-level<br>expectations such as (i) using correct verbs, tenses,<br>and pronouns/antecedents; (ii) using possessive<br>case (apostrophe -s) correctly; and, (iii) using<br>negatives and contractions correctly||
|ELPS 5.F|<br> <br> <br>write using a variety of grade-appropriate sentence<br>lengths, patterns, and connecting words to combine<br>phrases, clauses, and sentences in increasingly<br>accurate ways as more English is acquired|<br>p. 19, p. 35, p. 50, p. 69, p. 85, p. 122, p. 137, p. 154, p.<br>185, p. 200|
|ELPS 5.G|narrate, describe, and explain with increasing<br>specificity and detail to fulfill content area<br>writing needs as more English is acquired||
